1. PROJECT AND SECTOR BACKGROUND
Introduction
1.1   The province of Southeast Sulawesi (Sultra) covers a land area of 38,140 km2,
while Central Sulawesi (Sulteng) covers a much larger land area, about 63,689 km2. Sultra
has two Kabupatens which are part of the mainland Sulawesi Island. Two bigger islands,
Muna and Buton and some smaller islands make up its two remaining Kabupatens and
30 percent of its area. Most of Sulteng is on the main Sulawesi Island but the province
also has two medium island groups, the Banggai and Togian Islands, making up only
about 6 percent of the land area. Sultra's population was estimated at 1.5 million in 1994
with an average density of about 39 persons/km2. The province has one of the highest
population growth rates in the country, at about 3.7 percent per annum stemming from the
fact that transmigration has been an important part of the province's recent past (the
1980s). Sulteng had an estimated population of 1.9 million in 1994 and growing at about
2.8 percent per annum. Population density is very low (29 persons/km2) except in the
Palu valley where densities approach about 790 persons/km2.
1.2   Despite recent spurts of economic growth both provinces have low rural incomes
and welfare levels. The population is generally sparse and unevenly distributed with the
majority, 84 percent in Sultra and 85 percent in Sulteng, living in rural areas. The
provinces of Southeast and Central Sulawesi are characterized by immense cultural
diversity. In Sultra the major ethnic groups are Tolaki, the Tomekongga, and the
Tomoronene on the peninsula, and the Wolio, Munese and Butonese on the Islands of
Kabaena, Muna and Buton. In Sulteng there are reportedly 12 different ethnic groups and
possibly twice the number of linguistic groups. The major ethnic groups include the Kaili,
the Pamona, the Saluan, the Belantak, and the Banggai. Both provinces are now also
home to migrants from other parts of Sulawesi such as the Buginese, the Makasarese, the
Mandarese, the Gorontalos, the Manados, and the Bajaus as well as transmigrants from
Java, Bali and Lombok. These transmigrants in 1990 made up a non-negligible part of the
population of the two provinces (11 percent in Sultra and 6 percent in Sulteng).
1.3   Southeast and Central Sulawesi are two of Indonesia's provinces whose social
indicators are not as good as national average (Table 1.1 below). In each province the
estimated total population below the poverty line is above the national average. Both have
low life expectancy levels even among the Eastern provinces, higher infant mortality rates,
(surpassed only by NTB and East Timor in the East), high level of mal-nourishment and a
relatively low literacy level. Health statistics indicate that the areas served per health
center are on the high side especially for two relatively small provinces and hence
availability of health facilities appears to be poor.

Macroeconomic Background
1.4    Regional incomes, as measured by the Gross Regional Domestic Product excluding
oil and gas (GRDP), have been growing at reasonably high rates for the two provinces
since the early 1980s. The average annual GRDP growth rates were 7.2 percent for
Central and 8.5 percent (one of the highest in the country) for Southeast Sulawesi
between 1983-1992. These compare with a national GDP (without oil and gas) growth
rate of about 7.4 percent for the same period. Though GRDP growth rate in Central
Sulawesi (Sulteng) over 1983-92 was about equal to the average rate for the Eastern
provinces, the higher than average population growth rate of the province led to a per
capita GRDP growth rate below the regional average during the period.
1.5    The structure of the economy in Sulteng seemed to have changed rapidly between
1976 and 1983 when the share of agriculture fell from about 57 percent of GRDP to
42 percent. After 1983 however, the economic structure of the province changed very
little with agriculture maintaining its dominant role in the province's economy. As of 1992
agriculture's share of output was still 44 percent and employed about 68 percent of the
labor force. Southeast Sulawesi (Sultra) recorded one of the highest average GRDP
growth rates in Indonesia but it remains well behind other provinces in the country in
terms of the level of development. As of 1992, agriculture still dominated economic
activity in Sultra with a share of GRDP of about 44 percent and employing about
76 percent of the labor force. In both provinces, industry accounted for only a limited
share of provincial output and employment. Its contribution to output in 1992 was about
6 percent in Sulteng and 2.4 percent in Sultra. It also employed about 5.5 percent of the
labor force in Sulteng and 4 percent in Sultra.
1.6    Exports. As of 1991, the principal exports from Sulteng in value terms were
furniture and wooden construction materials (US$5.0 million), forest products (US$3.9
million) in the form of sawn timber (mostly ebony) and rattan, coconut products in the
form of oil and copra (US$2.8 million), and sea products US$0.9 million. While the main
exports from Sulteng have been from the agricultural sector, Sultra's principal exports



3
have been from the mining sector. In 1991 mining exports (mostly nickel) were US$52.4
million, compared to US$2.7 million for fishery products and US$ 1.7 million in forestry
products, mostly rattan.
The Agricultural Sector
1.7   The agricultural sector is the largest sector in the economy of both Southeast and
Central Sulawesi and provides the greatest source of employment. Growth in the
provincial economies has been led to a large degree by the agriculture sectors whose
growth rates averaged 7.7 percent per annum in Central and 8.0 percent per annum in
Southeast Sulawesi over 1983-92. These growth rates were considerably higher than the
3.2 percent national average growth rate of the sector during the same period. The
structures of the agricultural sectors of the two provinces are surprisingly very similar.
Within the agriculture sector, the food crops and estate crops sectors are dominant in both
provinces contributing approximately 75 percent of agricultural GRDP in 1992, with
about 40 percent of agricultural value-added coming from farm foodcrops, 35 percent
from the smallholder treecrop subsector. The contributions from the other subsectors were
10 percent from livestock, 12 percent from fisheries and about 3 percent from forestry.
1.8   The foodcrop subsector is still relatively large in both provinces and has been
growing annually at an average of about 5.9 percent and 4.4 percent respectively in
Central and Southeast Sulawesi. Paddy is the most widely planted food crop in both
provinces, but the area planted in Central Sulawesi (149,700 ha) is significantly larger
than the area planted in Southeast Sulawesi (74,100 ha). Major investments in irrigation
facilities in both provinces combined with more intensive extension efforts for paddy have
resulted in increases in production. However, average yields per hectare (3.3 tons) are
about 1.4 tons less than the national average and there is potential to further increase
output. Maize is the dominant staple food in some parts of Southeast Sulawesi (Muna and
Buton) and the total area planted is reported to be just over 48,500 ha in this province
while in Central Sulawesi only about 19,800 hectares are planted annually. Legume crops
have been increasing in importance as compared to paddy and maize. Of the legume
crops, soybeans are the most widely planted in both provinces (9,700 ha in Sultra and
14,600 ha in Sulteng). Other food sources, mainly sago, a food staple for some ethnic
groups, are widely harvested from swampy areas in parts of both provinces. Cassava is
also widely planted as a staple crop although it is less favored for consumption than rice.
1.9   In general, the smallholder treecrops subsectors in both provinces offer significant
potential to increase farmer incomes and to contribute to conservation of land resources.
While the subsector's contribution to agricultural GRDP did not change very much in
Sulteng between 1983 (32.4 percent) and 1992 (34.5 percent), it rose rapidly from 15
percent to 35 percent in Sultra during the same period. In Sulteng, coconut is the most
widely planted treecrop (164,390 ha) followed by cloves (51,500 ha), cocoa (29,900 ha),
The review in the next two sections is derived for the most part from the recent sector study on Eastern Islands
"Eastern Islands Rural Sector Strategy Review", Yellow Cover Report, November 1994



4
and coffee (17,400 ha). In Sultra, cashew nuts are the most widely cultivated treecrop
(123,900 ha), followed by cocoa (76,300 ha), coconuts (54,400 ha) and coffee (14,600
ha).
1.10  In Central Sulawesi, though the size of forestry output in total agricultural
production is still relatively small (3.2 percent in 1992), forestry sector output increased
faster than any other subsector at an average rate of over 13 percent per annum between
1983 and 1992. There appears however to be a problem of correctly monitoring forestry
exploitation because of a lack of facilities and personnel of the forestry service. For this
sector to continue to make a useful contribution to the province's economy, it is necessary
to carefully study, monitor, and manage the forests and critical watersheds as part of
forestry production and also in the agricultural and industrial development effort of the
province.
1.11   Livestock and especially fisheries are growth areas in the two provinces that have
good potential and for which market outlets exist outside the province and can make a
useful contribution to poverty reduction. In Sulteng, the average annual growth rate of
the fisheries sector over 1983-92 was 12 percent while for Sultra the rate was 11 percent.
In both provinces, fisheries contribute significantly to exports and directly support over 6
percent of all households in the two provinces. 2 The respective livestock sector growth
rates were 10 percent for Sulteng and 6 percent for Sultra.
Constraints to Agricultural Development
1.12   Natural and Physical Constraints. Most of the Sulawesi peninsula is
mountainous. The general terrain of Central Sulawesi and the northern part of Southeast
Sulawesi is characterized by a narrow coastal plain often only a few kilometers in width
and then steeply rugged mountain terrain still covered with forest. Central Sulawesi is a
crescent-shaped province whose economic, political and social structure is heavily
influenced by its geography. It is located on the equator, with high, steep elevations and
dense forest cover. The mountainous region and poor infrastructure have led to scattered
settlements and concentrations of population in the relatively more accessible but few
coastal plains, such as the Palu Valley with some consequences on the environment in such
areas. The soils in the northern part of the province are of volcanic origin, generally
fertile and well-drained and are very suitable for the production of estate crops. In the rest
of the province, the soils are of sedimentary and limestone origin, often less fertile than the
volcanic soils but still suited to treecrop production. Southeast Sulawesi has a generally
hilly and mountainous topography and only limited lowlands especially towards the north.
South of the east-west Kendari-Kolaka line the land is flatter and more open. Soils are
predominantly podsolic and Mediterranean, of limited fertility and, in certain parts, have
low water-holding capacity.
2   Eastern Islands Rural Sector Strategy Review, Chap 5 Pg. 99.



5
1.13  Land use data show that forests cover about 72 percent of the land area in Sulteng
and 67 percent in Sultra and only about 10.3 percent in both provinces is used for
agriculture and settlements. In many upland areas however shifting cultivation and
extensive farming practices have expanded, resulting in soil erosion and loss of soil
fertility. Both provinces have areas of high rainfall, but also some of the lowest rainfall
areas in Indonesia. The general range of rainfall is between 800 to 3,000 mm except for
the Palu area and southern Muna and Buton where annual precipitation of as low as 400
mm have been recorded. The generally mountainous topography of the provinces results
in a diversity of localized micro-climates with unevenly distributed rainfall patterns during
the year, placing a constant strain on rainfed production.
1.14  Levels of Technology. In general, levels of productivity in the agricultural sectors
of the two provinces are below the national averages. There is therefore a significant
potential to increase yields for all commodities both foodcrops and treecrops through
introduction and dissemination of improved varieties and improved farming practices.
Major problems to be overcome in increasing average yields include: (a) inadequate
availability of improved seeds and seedlings for all commodities; (b) low demand for
fertilizers for crops other than paddy stemming from ineffective extension programs
introducing and explaining their use; (c) inadequate availability of and poor construction
of irrigation facilities especially in the smaller irrigated areas; (d) inadequate extension
programs to introduce effective pest management methods (including for vertebrate
pests); and (e) inadequate post-harvest practices and storage facilities. For dryland
cultivation, though population densities are low in the two provinces, the fragile nature of
the soils demands greater attention to preserve shallow top soils and reduce the
deterioration in soil fertility. This requires a change in the farming pattern of slash and
burn and shifting cultivation. The need for the introduction of conservation farming
techniques (hitherto not practiced) is therefore as important as in overcrowded Java and
Bali.
1.15  Infrastructure. Sultra remains one of the remotest provinces of Indonesia though
it has fairy good air and sea access to outside regions of South Sulawesi and Maluku.
Sulteng on the other hand is relatively more accessible. Within both provinces
transportation remains a key constraint. Paved roads connect most of the major towns but
most of them are in a state of disrepair. There remains therefore considerable scope for
further improving the state of the major road network. Regular ferry services and in some
cases "speed" boats connect the mainland to the major islands and Kabupatens and also to
the more remote islands and is the major means of transport in the region. Within each
province however there are numerous access roads, village paths, and tracks that are
impassable during the rainy season. In a lot of these areas, access by motor vehicles and
carts is constrained and frequently farmers must carry all inputs and output. Improvement
of these roads, as well as the main network of roads, would reduce transport costs and
boost marketing of agricultural produce.
1.16 Policies and Incentives Affecting Agricultural Production. National level trade
deregulation measures over the last five years have strongly impacted the treecrop



6
subsector in both Southeast and Central Sulawesi. Following the deregulation packages
of the early 1990s removing trade restrictions on coffee, copra and cocoa, the markets for
these crops have become quite competitive, with farmers receiving remunerative prices
and smallholder investments in these crops increasing sharply. 3 Under the general guise of
raising local revenues, there are still a number of local and provincial taxes (or retribusi)
imposed on the transport of certain commodities and animals and more especially on inter-
regional trade. Though these are generally of a nuisance value, their effect on production
and farmer incentives appears to be negligible. Despite the existence of a national quota
system on inter-provincial livestock trade, the quota system is not enforced in Sultra and
Sulteng and hence has no effect on livestock prices.
1.17  In some isolated cases, local district and even village authorities sometimes restrict
competition by requiring produce to be sold through the local cooperative (KUD) or
licensed traders. Following instructions from the Minister of Home Affairs (Keputusan
Menteri Dalam Negeri No. 48/1984, amended by KEPMEN No. 29/1986) supported by
instructions coming, from time to time, from the Governors to the Bupatis (e.g. Letter No.
512/1832/Ro. Ekon of 7 May 1994 from Governor of Central Sulawesi to all 4 Bupatis),
there doesn't appear to be any more unusual local marketing restrictions or any new
attempts by local authorities to limit competition by requiring produce to be sold through
the local cooperative (KUD) or only licensed traders. Inputs (especially fertilizer) continue
to be sold through the cooperatives and a limited number of licensed traders. This is
however related to the national fertilizer subsidy program and the policy of PUSRI, the
fertilizer company, to use only designated distributors. This system however does not
appear to affect the availability or price of inputs in the major centers and farmers who
have the means to acquire inputs should be able to get them either from Kabupaten or
from some kecamatan centers. The production of stock seed from the Kabupaten Seed
Centers (BBP) and extension seed from farmer seed growers provide less than half ofthe total
seed requirements of farmers who now rely mainly on low yielding local and degenerated HYV
varieties. Any new programs to improve agricultural productivity will therefore have to
address the seed supply issue.
1.18  Capital Availability and Investments. The public sector continues to make the
largest contribution to investments in the two provinces through the development budgets
(APBN, APBD I and APBD II). Of the total development expenditures, an average of
about 70 percent in Central Sulawesi and about 65 percent in Southeast Sulawesi came
from the national development budget APBN, though the share of the two local
development budgets had been increasing over the latter part of the Repelita V period.
The total development budgets for both Southeast and Central Sulawesi more than
doubled between the first and last years of Repelita V. They rose from about Rp. 99.6
billion for Southeast Sulawesi and Rp. 159.6 billion for Central Sulawesi in 1989/90 to
about Rp 221 billion and Rp 450 billion respectively in 1993/94. These compare to an
3   "Indonesia: Sulawesi 's Cocoa Boom - Lessons of Smallholder Dynamism and Hands Off Policy" April 1995.
Informal ESW Report EA3AG. Paper presented at Deregulation Conference; also Indonesia's Smallholder
Coffee - Benefits of Deregulation and Risks of Re-Regulation. Informal ESW EA3AG June 1995



7
average estimated annual investment of around Rp 6 billion per province (2 percent and 1
percent of the development budgets of the two provinces) to be made under the proposed
project.
1.19  In general, infrastructure and transportation has received the bulk of the
development expenditures, especially at the local level. Agriculture's share in the national
(APBN) and provincial (APBD I) development budgets has been slightly above 10
percent. At the Kabupaten level however the share of agriculture and irrigation in the
development budget (APBD II) has averaged less than 4 percent for the two provinces. In
1993/94, the annual development budget for agriculture from APBN and APBD TK I & II
had risen to Rp. 35 billion for Central Sulawesi and to Rp 48 billion for Southeast
Sulawesi. On an annual basis, the proposed project investment would represent about 16
and 12.5 percent respectively of the annual agricultural development expenditures of the
two provinces. Private sector investments in treecrop production over the last several
years has been quite high though there is no data on the levels. Approved domestic and
foreign investments have in general fluctuated significantly from year to year but over time
have made up less than 0.9 percent of all domestic investment approvals in the country (11
percent of Eastern Indonesia) and less than 0.2 percent of all foreign investment approvals
(3 percent of the approvals in Eastern Indonesia).
1.20  Agricultural Credit. In the two provinces, transactions through the banking
system are very limited. The value added of the banking and financial services sector
remains very small, making up less than 1 percent of GRDP in Southeast and less than 2
percent in Central Sulawesi. Most banks, at best, only have branches at the Kabupaten
level and many of the promising rural financial service programs that have operated in the
past and still operate in the country, including the relatively more successful Unit Desas
(Kupedes) and P4K programs of the BRI (Bank Rakyat Indonesia) have operated only
marginally in the two provinces if at all. I Access of farmers and other small-scale
investors to financial resources therefore remains an important constraint for investment
and they cannot rely on the formal banking and financial system to provide their credit
needs. To avoid these problems of lack of access to credit facilities, sectoral projects
often come with their own arrangements and mechanisms for input supply and some
credit. As development projects and programs become regionalized, it would become
necessary to explore alternative forms of rural finance mechanisms for the rural
population, especially in the Eastern Islands. Experiences from Indonesia (Yogya Upland
Project, Integrated Swamps project) and elsewhere (Albania Rural Poverty Alleviation
Project, Benin Rural Savings and Loan Project) show that community-based savings and
credit systems can provide useful alternatives.
1.21 Institutional Constraints. Government's current policy aims at gradually
decentralizing responsibility for development to the local levels. Though there are
adequate numbers of local government staff at both the provincial and kabupaten levels,
there is a general lack of sufficiently trained and experienced staff to carry out the level of
4  Eastern Islands Rural Sector Strategy Review, Chap 3



8
responsibilities that would accompany full and effective decentralization. Another
important constraint for regional planning and implementing development, concerns the
willingness and capacity of the local staff to take initiatives. Because the staff in the
regions have mostly implemented centrally managed programs, tend to have a lower level
of education, and limited experience, they are not accustomed to taking initiatives in
planning and targeting development. A review of institutional capacity of key government
and other agencies during project preparation identified the following constraints:
* limited formal and on-the-job training opportunities as well as inadequate
applied training;
* inadequate provision of equipment and facilities and low levels of discretionary
development funding at the kabupaten to support rural development activities
effectively;
* lack of reliable and relevant data on the regional (sub-district, district and/or
provincial) economy for the purposes of proper development planning.



9
2. BANK INVOLVEMENT AND LESSONS LEARNED
Experience with Area Development Operations.
2.1    Bank Wide Experience. 5 The Bank has supported a large number of area
development projects, 45 percent of them in Africa. These projects, however, fell into
disfavor in the mid-1980s following the evaluated poor performance of the earlier
projects. In several countries, a series of projects had been launched in quick succession,
leaving little time for learning and applying lessons from experience. Most evaluations, by
the Bank as well as other donors, associated poor performance of these projects with
project complexity and lack of flexibility, inadequate attention to village level institution
building and beneficiary participation, poor policy and incentive framework, and lack of
appropriate production technology, institutional capacity and government commitment.
Notwithstanding the poor track record, some area development projects have been quite
successful. OED studies have concluded that with good local management and
appropriate policy framework, this type of project can be very effective.
2.2   Indonesian Experience. Area development type projects in Indonesia have been
implemented with varying degrees of success and impact on poverty alleviation. Lack of
sustainable technological packages for land and crop development, shortages of family
labor, inadequate agricultural support services and poor inter-agency coordination were
major constraints to realization of the development objectives of the swamp development
projects, the transmigration projects, and the two upland area development projects
(Yogyakarta Rural Development and the Upland Agriculture and Conservation projects)
financed by the Bank. The Project Performance Audit Reports (PPAR) for three of the
five transmigration projects concluded that while the resettlement objectives of these
projects were satisfactorily achieved, the planned agricultural development necessary to
generate income and employment for the transmigrants was deficient. 6 The first Bank-
financed non-transmigration related area development project in Indonesia, the
Yogyakarta Rural Development project, was also judged unsatisfactory by the PPAR on
grounds of over-ambitious project objectives, faulty project design, and non-replicable and
non-sustainable land development technology. 7
2.3   To some extent these problems were addressed in the design and/or during
implementation of the second generation projects such as the Nusa Tenggara Agricultural
Support Project (NTASP; 1986), and the Yogyakarta Upland Area Development Project
(YUADP; 1991), resulting in their better implementation and development impact. These
s  OED Lessons Series: "Lessons & Practices 3 - Area Development Projects". Also OED Precis 56: Evaluation
Results for '92 - The Agriculture Porfolio
6  OED Report No 12874 - Impact Evaluation Report of Indonesia's Transmigration Projects
7  OED Report No. 11981 PPAR of Indonesia - Yogyakarta Rural Development Project



10
projects have made important contributions to the development of local level institutions,
processes for decentralized planning, implementation of development activities, and
experimented with approaches for beneficiary participation and use of project area CDOs.
The NTASP and the UACP have been generating location specific upland and arid/semi-
arid farming technologies, and fostering the much needed operational linkages between
research and extension in the project areas.
2.4   Lessons Learnt. The main lessons learnt from the implementation of the two area
development projects in Indonesia, NTASDP and YUADP, and as reflected in their
Implementation Completion Reports are consistent with those from some 300 such Bank
wide projects. These show that to succeed, area development projects need: (a) clear and
unequivocal long-term political commitment to and policy support for rural development
and poverty alleviation objectives; (b) beneficiary participation in the identification,
planning and implementation process in order to ensure their 'ownership' of the project;
(c) realistic, simple and flexible project design based on objective diagnosis of
development constraints and their possible solutions; (d) proven technology and support
services for production growth, or effective measures for technology development and
their adoption during project implementation; (e) demonstrated institutional capability at
the local level for project implementation, or effective measures to strengthen existing
institutions early in project implementation; (f) routine monitoring and periodic evaluation
of project implementation and impact, as a basis for 'mid-course' corrections; and (g) to
avoid the involvement of excessive number of agencies and satisfactory inter-agency
coordination where integrated/simultaneous implementation of several different
components are necessary for synergistic benefits. In addition, experience has shown that
sustainability of development impact is enhanced if the project structure is integrated with
standard local development administration, rather that a super-imposed specialized project
administration. The proposed project will incorporate these lessons through simple and
flexible design and by limiting activities principally to the agricultural sector. Mid-Term
evaluation will also be an important feature of the project for project redesign in response
to any changed circumstances.
Government's Development Strategy.
2.5    Government's overall strategy for growth, poverty alleviation and regional
development has had the following key elements: (a) fostering rapid growth of the major
income and employment generating sectors of the economy: agriculture, industry, and
services; (b) promoting rural urban linkages and migration out of overpopulated and
resource constrained areas; and (c) investing in physical infrastructure and human resource
development. For reasons of social equity and regional balance however, the Government
of Indonesia (GOI) has placed particular emphasis on the development of the eastern
islands during the Second Development Period, PJP II, and during REPELITA VI. GOI
strategy for the East emphasizes human resource and physical infrastructure development,
productivity gains in agriculture, agribusiness and small scale village processing, and



11
integrated area development activities. The specific strategy for the project areas is similar
to the rest of the East and puts emphasis on:
*  agricultural development, with particular emphasis on productivity increases,
agri-business, development of potential irrigation sites, and improvements in
agricultural services;
*  infrastructure development, particularly road transportation, facilities for inter-
island transport, and communications infrastructure;
*  human resource development;
*  industrial development, particularly in the form of agro-processing and mining;
and
*  deconcentration of governmental activities and some decentralization of
decision making, giving increasing powers to the local level institutions on
development administration;
2.6    The last element of the strategy dealing with decentralization is particularly
important for Government's regional development program. It is expected to be linked
with increased general purpose transfers that would permit provinces and districts to
finance their development programs without restrictions from the center and permit them
to provide counterpart funds directly for externally funded projects. Two elements of
government's strategy that is important for poverty alleviation and hence relevant to the
Eastern regional development effort is the recently instituted INPRES Desa Tertinggal
(IDT), designed specifically to target villages that are at or below the poverty level and the
integrated area development program (PPWT) sponsored by the Ministry of Home Affairs
through its regional development directorate, BANGDA.
2.7    Regional Development Objectives. Regional development objectives in both
Southeast and Central Sulawesi as contained in their respective regional development
plans, reflect those at the national level and emphasize: (i) achieving sustainable economic
growth; (ii) reducing the incidence of poverty through broad-based income generation;
and (iii) promoting a more equitable pattern of growth to reduce inter-regional disparities.
In both of these provinces, the key regional development priorities are:
rural and community development;
improvements in agricultural productivity;
development of agro-industry and agri-business;
roads and infrastructure development to support regional development;
natural resource conservation; and
human resource development.



12
Investments from both the public and private sectors are expected to be directed to these
areas during REPELITA VI to achieve the targeted growth.
Bank's Strategy
2.8    Agricultural Sector Strategy. The main objectives of the Bank's evolving
strategy in the agricultural sector are to ensure agricultural growth with distributional
equity, and to assist in rural poverty alleviation through income generation.8 To achieve
this, the Bank envisages an increased emphasis on regional operations, designed: (a) to
adapt development programs to the specific constraints and conditions of each area or
region involved; (b) to improve and focus provision of agricultural services (extension,
research, input distribution, etc.) in needed areas; (c) to elicit an increased local
participation in planning and implementation of operations; and (d) to strengthen local
institutions to be effective in assisting farmers in increasing their incomes and in evaluating
development effectiveness.
2.9    While agriculture is unlikely to be a major source of growth in the Indonesian
economy as a whole in the near future, growth in the sector is still a key element of
development and poverty alleviation strategy in provinces such as those in the proposed
project regions where a high percentage of the population depends on agriculture and
where 40 to 50 percent of regional GDP still comes from agriculture. Thus the Bank's
focus would be to develop agricultural operations which raise productivity, rural incomes
and reduce poverty in the regions as a complement to sector-wide investments, national
policy reforms, and institutional change. In the remote, resource-poor regions where
poverty is more entrenched, such as the Eastern Islands, the Bank's strategy would be to
implement carefully designed and special interventions addressing issues of sustainable
improvements in farm incomes and productivity, and off-farm incomes where possible.
Project design also seeks to ensure appropriate support to non-agricultural activities that
directly affect agricultural production. The project would thus include activities, such as
village infrastructure that would support the rural development process and poverty
alleviation.
2.10   Country Assistance Strategy and Rationale for Bank Involvement. The above
strategy and proposals included in the proposed project are consistent with the Bank's
Country Assistance Strategy for Indonesia, discussed in the meeting of Executive
Directors held on March 21, 1995, supports the Government in the following key areas:
(a) maintaining growth with macro-economic stability; (b) enhancing competitiveness; (c)
reducing poverty; (d) enhancing human resource development; and (e) managing
resources sustainably. The proposed project reflects the more decentralized region-
specific approach that is now emerging and is consistent with the Bank assistance
strategy's basic approaches for poverty alleviation and decentralization of development
activities. The project has a poverty-reduction emphasis, promotes sustainable food
8   Report No. 10504-lND "Indonesia: Agricultural Transformation Challenges and Opportunities" Chap 6. Also
Eastern Islands Rural Sector Strategy



13
security, and enhances competitiveness in areas such as tree crops where the provinces
have a comparative advantage. This strategy is consistent with GOI's approach to
agricultural development. The Bank's broad experience in the agriculture sector in
Indonesia, its projects in the eastern islands, in particular the recently completed Nusa
Tenggara project (NTASP) and the adaptive research program just starting under the
second Agriculture Research Management (ARM 2) project, its sector work (Eastern
Island Rural Sector Strategy Study of 1994), and its complementary lending program in
eastern Indonesia, emphasizing rural and market infrastructure, town development and
community health and nutrition, put the Bank in a unique position to provide assistance
for the region's agricultural development effort.
2.11   The proposed project complements ongoing Bank-financed infrastructure and
human resources development projects in the region. In particular, the Eastern Indonesia
Kabupaten Roads Project (Loan 3579-IND, 1993, US$155 million); the Water Supply and
Sanitation for Low Income Communities Project (Loan 3629-IND, 1993, US$80 million);
the Third Health Project (Loan 3042-IND, 1989, US$43.5 million); and the Third
Nutrition and Community Health Project (Loan 3550-IND, 1993, US$93.5 million)
address basic human needs and improve linkages between farms and markets. The
proposed Sulawesi Towns Assistance and Development Project is also expected to
provide key complementary marketing infrastructure in the major towns of the project
area. This would support the agricultural production activities of the proposed project.
The Government's strong focus on infrastructure in the region will also assist the strategy.
2.12  The Second Agricultural Research Management Project (ARM II, Loan 3886-
IND, 1995, US$63.0 million) will strengthen farming systems research throughout the
country through decentralized budgeting, local control and identifying and promoting
technologies appropriate to the local ecosystems. The ARM II Project will finance eight
regional Assessment Institutes including the two in the project area, one at Biromaru (to
cover North and Central Sulawesi) and the other at Kendari (to cover Southeast and
South Sulawesi). These institutes are expected to generate location specific technologies
that will provide a strong technical underpinning for agricultural development being
promoted under the proposed project.



14
3. THE PROJECT
Project Origin
3.1    Historically, Bank support for the agricultural sector has been through projects
financed through central sectoral agencies - sectoral operations with components in the
provinces. A number of these operations have and continue to have a positive impact on
poverty reduction and the growth of the regions, especially when they have financed
irrigation and other infrastructure or sought to transfer know-how and technology from
one area to the other, and where central coordination is required. The recent Eastern
Islands Rural Sector Strategy Study however strongly argued for area development
operations that would not only complement the sector projects but would seek to improve
the impact of development operations by: (a) reflecting specific local conditions (e.g.,
farming systems, soil conditions, etc.) in project design; (b) taking into account the
immediate priorities of the regions; and (c) permitting a high level of beneficiary
participation in planning and implementation. 9 The proposed project responds to this
recommendation from the study.
Project Objectives
3.2   The overall objectives of the project are to assist Government in reducing the
incidence of poverty in the provinces of Central and Southeast Sulawesi through the
increase of rural incomes, the promotion of equitable regional development, the promotion
of environmentally sustainable farming practices, and the strengthening of local level
institutions. Its specific project objectives are:
*  to increase farmer productivity and incomes;
* to promote environmentally sustainable farming practices; and
* to improve the capacity of local institutions to identify, prepare, appraise and
manage agricultural development programs.
Project Target Areas
3.3    A number of project target areas and activities have been selected by local
government authorities based on inputs from the rural population in the bottom-up
planning process that went into the preparation of Repelita VI. Other priority areas and
activities were also added following field discussions during preparation. The main criteria
that guided the selection of project locations were:
a) to reach a large number of households below or near the poverty line,
9   Eastem Islands Report op. cit. Chap. 2



15
b) to provide assistance to areas with significant potential for agricultural
development that have hitherto been neglected or only marginally been touched
by other operations;
c) to cover areas identified as requiring special attention in the provincial and
district Repelita VI; and
d) areas where there is a minimum level of government support services as well as
infrastructure to connect them to regional centers.
3.4   The proposed project will be implemented in all eight Kabupatens of the provinces
of Southeast and Central Sulawesi, with particular focus on the lesser developed
Kecamatans. The target areas selected in Central Sulawesi are located in 27 Kecamatans
with a total land area of some 22,500 sq. km (35 percent of the land area) and covering
585 villages of which 286 or 49 percent have been classified as poor. The target area has
an estimated population of 625,000 people (129,000 households) or 33 percent of the
province's population . The target areas in Southeast Sulawesi are in 25 Kecamatans,
with a land area of 22,000 sq. km (58 percent of the area) and would involve some 289
villages of which about 160 or 55 percent are classified poor. The estimated population of
the target area is 426,000 (84,100 households) or 31 percent of the total population. The
target areas in general, are outside of the traditional areas that receive donor assistance
around Kendari and Palu.
Detailed Project Features
3.5   The project has three broad components covering: (a) agriculture-based area
development; (b) capacity strengthening and project management; and (c) farming systems
and fisheries research support and technology transfer.
Component A: Agriculture-Based Area Development (US$ 26.4 million; 80 percent
of total base cost)
3.6   This component aims to improve agricultural productivity, incomes and levels of
welfare of rural households in the 54 Kecamatans by implementing a program of
agricultural development activities. A secondary objective would be to improve the pattern
of agricultural land use and reduce soil erosion and loss of soil fertility from traditional
farming practices. Several activities (described below) have been identified to constitute a
core menu from which the specific activities for each area have been proposed, taking into
account the area's resource base. The menu identified includes: improved cropping in
village irrigation schemes, dryland farming systems development, cattle distribution,
household technology packages, and vaccination of chickens against Newcastle disease.
In addition, the project would also support the improvement of critical road sections,
bridges and culverts to improve communications between farms and markets, and includes



16
an as yet unidentified component of other development activities to be proposed by local
communities during implementation.
3.7   The agricultural development activity in each area would consist of direct input
and equipment support for demonstration plots in farmers' fields, and basic extension and
financial support for replication in the farming activities included in the menu. A village
level revolving credit system (following a successful model developed in an Albania
operation and also a similar one in the ongoing Yogyakarta Upland Project) will be set up
to provide the financial support for the replication program. Annual work programs and
budgets for each area would be based on a community-based planning system. Existing
women farmer groups (wanita tani) would have the same access to the village revolving
credit system, as well as any other project support available to other farmer groups.
3.8   Activities would be grouped into four sub-components: (a) Crop Development
program (including foodcrop and tree crop activities); (b) livestock production and
veterinary care; (c) Farm access roads; and (d) Community-based initiatives.
3.9   Crop Development Program. The crop development program would cover
foodcrop, horticulture and treecrop production systems. The main activities to be
financed would include:
(a) Irrigation Farming System. The project would finance improvement of
agricultural practices on village irrigation schemes where water control
structures are being constructed under the Governments crash Village
Irrigation Program (PID). The project would support the establishment of
demonstration plots of 3 ha each in adjacent 0.5 ha farm plots involving six
farmers in each of the 203 irrigation schemes being rehabilitated in the project
area, and support improved seed multiplication for rice, soybean, mungbean
and maize. This would average about 2 ha seed multiplication units for each 3
schemes for a total of 20 seed units in Southeast and about 48 units for
Central. In addition the project includes extension support for improved
irrigation cultivation (Annex 1 Section A). Farmers would be able to obtain
credit under the village revolving credit scheme for replication (Annex 1
Section I).
(b) Dryland Farming. The project would finance the introduction and extension
support for the promotion of conservation (dryland) farming technology for
upland farmers using alley-cropping, bench terracing, contour ridge terracing
(covering treecrops, fruit trees and foodcrops), soil conservation techniques,
and rehabilitation of smallholder treecrop plantations. Some of these dryland
technology packages have recently been introduced in some of the Eastern
Island provinces such as NTB and NTT though they are more widely practiced
in the uplands of the more heavily populated Java and Bali. The technology
packages are also practiced in small pockets of Sulawesi with support from
community development organizations (CDO). To introduce the packages, the



17
project would support establishment of some 90 conservation farming
demonstration plots of 3 ha each in Southeast and 210 plots of 3 ha each in
Central Sulawesi."' (Annex 1 Section C)
(c) Treecrop Rehabilitation. The project would also provide for demonstration
of rehabilitation of existing overgrown cashew and cacao farms. In each case
there will be 3 ha demonstration plots in the cacao or cashew villages. There
will be a total of 72 cacao demonstration plots (covering 216 ha) in Central
Sulawesi and 90 plots (270 ha) each of cacao and cashew demonstration in
Southeast Sulawesi. The project would also provide support for the
establishment of one small private treecrops nursery for every 3 villages to
provide improved seedling (Annex 1 Section E). The project also includes
arrangements for the provision of group credit to give farmers access to
liquidity they can use to finance inputs they would need to replicate the new
technology.
(d) Household Plots. The component includes promotion of packages through
extension support and demonstration activities to upgrade existing household
technologies (such as house gardens, improved hearths, drying floors etc.),
which are traditionally the responsibilities of women (Annex 1 Section F).
3.10  In order to ensure that farmers who want to adopt and replicate project activities
and project introduced technology have the means to do so, the project would finance a
village-level revolving credit fund that would make liquidity available to farmer groups for
input purchase, crop marketing and agricultural processing. Government has agreed at
negotiations to pass on the credit as a grant to villages. Despite the fact that funds will be
a grant to participating villages, farmer groups and individual farmers would have to repay
the credit with a minimum interest rate of 15 percent per year in the initial years and a
higher rate in the latter years so that the fund continues to serve village members in the
future. Individual communities will however be able to charge higher rates if they wish.
Each participating village would receive an initial revolving credit that would not exceed
Rp 10 million for on-lending to villagers, farmers, and farmer groups in the village. For
replication credits the technical services (Dinas/PPLs) would need to assure themselves
that the groups in question are adopting the recommended technology. Repayment would
be after the planting season and would be made to a special account to be opened by each
village at the nearest institution providing deposit services, expected to be either a
commercial bank or post office (pos giro). While the basic objective of the program would
be to ensure that farmers are able to replicate the agricultural programs, it was agreed that up
to 40 percent of the village funds can be on-lent for agricultural processing and marketing to be
undertaken by the villagers or groups within the village. The main criteria and procedures
agreed on for the operation of the program during negotiations are summarized above, and are
given in detail in Annex 1 Section I.
10  This implies that there would be a demonstration plot established in one out of every three villages in the
project area.



18
3.11   Livestock Management and Veterinary Care. This program would include:
distribution of breeding cattle and bulls through a revolving credit system, in priority to
farmers who have successfully participated in the crop production program of
conservation farming and village irrigation programs; advice to farmers to establish plots
for fodder crops; veterinary programs through improvements to existing animal health
clinics; and provision of vaccines and other materials to cover vaccination of an average of
about 700 chicken each in all project villages. Receipts from the sale of the vaccines is
expected to set up a revolving credit to cover the chicken vaccination program for
prevention of Newcastle disease. The program is expected to be managed by the existing
women's group, PKK (Annex I Sections H & G).
3.12   As recommended in the Eastern Islands sector study " management of the
redistribution of cattle and of the cattle revolving credit would be done by the community.
Sub-Kecamatan livestock committees L2/ are to be set up (regrouping heads of villages and
representatives of farmer groups) to be responsible for ensuring recuperation of the
livestock credit and redistribution of second generation cattle to farmers who have not yet
received the cattle and who meet the criteria to receive it. The committees would be
assisted by a working group for livestock distribution to be formed at each kecamatan and
to be composed of the Mantri Ternak, PPLs for livestock, staff of the animal health
clinics, village chiefs, and representatives of farmer groups, the kecamatan community
development facilitator (CDF), and staff of the Livestock Dinas TK II. Under the
supervision of the Dinas TK IIIPIMPRO, the livestock group would be responsible for
selecting the desa's and farmer groups to receive cattle and for cattle procurement.
3.13   Farm Access Roads. In order to reduce transportation bottlenecks, especially
from farm-to-market and between villages, the project would support the construction of
critical bridges, culverts, and road sections on village access roads. Local government
funding and community participation would be mobilized to improve the surfacing for
these access roads as necessary. Other village infrastructure such as simple village docks
and jetties, marketing and storage facilities may also be financed. Ideally, allocations for
each kecamatan and village should reflect their infrastructure needs. However, in order to
ensure that there is equity in providing the infrastructure and to ensure that local
participation will be maximized, an upper limit of some US$250,000 per kecamatan is
proposed. In order to get the maximum participation of the population in the road
construction program, it will be implemented by the village resilience group, LKMD as is
currently being done under the village infrastructure projects (VIP) being financed by the
Bank and OECF. Design and technical support would be provided by the Public Works
Dinas. A rural infrastructure construction manual, already prepared for the VIP project in
Bahasa Indonesia, will be adapted during the first project year and will be used for
implementing the component. A consultant rural engineer/contracts specialist will be
Il Eastern Islands Report op. cit Chap. 6
2 /One could consider a Kecamatan committee per every 8 to 10 villages with each committee having a
representative from one of the villages.



19
recruited by the project to oversee the construction program and help with procurement
and contracting. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that: (a) the infrastructure
construction manual will be finished by March 31, 1997, for use in the road construction
program; (b) allocations for the village infrastructure program, per kecamatan involved in
the project, will not exceed the equivalent of US$250,000; (c) priority would be given to
villages where needs are demonstrated to be the highest, the population is committed to
contribute actively to the implementation of the works, and local government has agreed
to maintain the completed works. The upper limit would be reviewed during the mid-term
review of the project (Annex 1 Section J).
3.14  Community Based Initiatives. The project would support local initiatives
expected to result in direct benefits to the communities. The sub-component would allow
village communities opportunities to propose priority activities that may not have been
included in the core menu of activities (post-harvest processing, goat distribution, spring-
fed village water supply, marketing and storage facilities, support for pilot village-run
credit schemes, etc.), or to permit further replication of particularly successful programs.
While the sub-component would seek to finance productive activities, it can also provide
other forms of support covering social-type (but non-religious) activities that can be
demonstrated to be in the manifest interest of the rural population, including providing
assess of women's and youth groups to CDO assistance, demonstration programs and
other project activities. Technically and economically feasible activities considered to be a
high priority by the local communities would be implemented using discretionary
supplemental project funding to be provided through the project. Though no specific limit
has been set, it is expected that each kecamatan would receive about Rp 90 million during
the project (equivalent to some Rp 22.5 million or US$10,000 annually for four years).
Community development activities are expected to be implemented with support from
CDOs. Procedures and criteria for selecting CDOs were discussed and agreed on during
negotiations and these are reported below in para 5.10.
3.15  Proposals would be made annually through the village level planning process and
with support from community development facilitators to be recruited under the project.
The village level proposals, formulated by farmer groups and/or villages with assistance
from the community development facilitators, will be consolidated at the kecamatan level
and reviewed to prioritize those with the greatest socio-economic impact and those
expected to improve rural incomes. The purpose of the review will be to ensure that
proposed activities meet the necessary criteria and to rank the activities coming from the
kecamatan by order of priority. The proposals will be forwarded to the Kabupaten, where
the project steering committee would review the proposals to ensure that they conform to
criteria for the component acceptable to the Bank and decide on those to be financed.
Assurances to this effect were obtained during negotiations. The criteria for financing
activities under this component, discussed and agreed on at negotiations, are detailed in
Annex 1 Section K. They include the following:
. activities have to be identified by the population to be of high priority;



20
*  the population has to demonstrate that the activity is in their manifest interest by
their willingness to contribute at least their labor to the realization of the activity;
* the population has to demonstrate their willingness to provide for the operation and
maintenance of activities financed under the sub-component.
*  activities involving women and youth groups
3.16  The Implementation Schedule for activities under the Crop Development Program
is displayed in Annex 7 Table 1.
Component B: Project Management and Capacity Strengthening of Local Agricultural
Support Services: (US$ 5.1 million; 15 percent of total base cost).
3.17  The large part of the component would finance activities related to project
implementation and management, but since the project will be managed by local agencies,
the component will also serve to strengthen the capacity of these local agricultural and
development institutions to plan, design, prepare, monitor, coordinate and evaluate a
multi-sectoral package of development activities. The proposed project envisages
financing of, training, equipment and vehicles (in particular motorcycles for field staff),
improvement of agency facilities, and provision of operating means for institutions
involved in the delivery of agricultural support services at the provincial and district levels.
3.18   Rehabilitation and Improvement of Facilities. The project would: rehabilitate a
total of 25 rural extension centers (BPPs) and 4 animal health clinics (poskeswans) in the
two provinces. There will not be any new construction either of BPPs orposkeswans.
However, in the rehabilitation program for the BPPs, for areas where there are no
poskeswans, the project would finance the refurbishing of an existing room or the addition
of a new room to provide animal health services. Assurances were obtained at
negotiations that before any of these facilities would be considered for rehabilitation under
the project, detailed management and operational plans would be implemented for the
facility covering the following:
*  staffing levels for the concerned BPP andlor poskeswan (both current and
anticipated) including where anticipated staff would come from;
*  current annual funding (operational and maintenance budget) and funding
requirements to support full and effective operation of the center or clinic;
*  coverage of the BPP/poskeswan in terms of area and number of farmers to be
reached;
*  for each BPP, principal or leading sector the BPP covers, and hence the
coordinating Dinas for the center. The plan should however include proposed
budgetary arrangements for ensuring that all participating sectors (in addition
to the leading sector) contribute to the functioning of the BPP; and
*  for each Poskeswan or its one-room extension, a program to ensure cost
recovery and budgeted maintenance of the clinic.



21
3.19 Kabupaten Seed Multiplication Centers. The Government of Indonesia, since
the introduction of the BIMAS (mass guidance) in the early sixties, has considered seed
production to be one of the five major aspects in foodcrops (especially rice) production
improvement. The provision of foundation seed has therefore traditionally been the
responsibility of research. First stage multiplication has normally been done by provincial
and kabupaten seed farms for distribution to farmers. The seed multiplication program has
been supported by various Government policies on seed certification. Private sector
involvement in this area has been virtually non-existent since the B1MAS subsidized seed
policy kept them out in a major way from seed production. However, the implementation
of the BIMAS seed policies has faced major constraints at provincial and kabupaten level,
so that the availability of extension seeds at the farming level is not sufficient, in particular
in the more remote areas in Indonesia, away from Java and Bali and the operation of
existing seed multiplication centers has been poor. The project would provide some funds
to rehabilitate and to support the operation of one seed center per Kabupaten to produce
stock seed, for multiplication by seed farmers, provided a management plan is prepared
and implemented for each center to be supported. The Plan would cover:
-  operational program to produce the required stock seed based on available
area;
o  cost of seed production and expected production;
o  proposals on how the produced stock seed will be marketed and expected sales
revenue;
o  proposals on origin of foundation seed,
3.20  The stock seed to be produced at these centers would be sold to seed farmers who
would multiply them for sale to other farmers or for their own use. The seed to be
produced at these centers would cover the major crops such as paddy, soybean, peanuts,
maize and mungbeans depending on farmers' demand.
3.21   Training, Workshops and Seminars. The project would support training in
management, organization, planning and technical activities for extension workers,
technical and other project staff. Training programs would include: (a) project
orientation and mid-term review workshops as well as training of provincial, Kabupaten
and field level staff on project and financial management, program planning, monitoring
and evaluation, gender sensitivity, environmental management, and community-based
development planning and implementation; (b) special leadership and organizational
training programs for farmers and village leaders on community-based development
activities, planning and farmer organization; and (c) continuous training of field level
extension staff on technical themes. The training program will also focus on helping field
extension workers identify technologies and agricultural production systems targeted for
women and the youth. To allow the extension and technical staff to do this, the project
will provide particular gender sensitivity training (in terms of the roles of different
household members) to project staff



22
3.22 Technical Assistance. The project would finance:
(a)   about 350 staff-months of local consultancy support for various activities,
including: program coordinator (48 staff-months); area development
specialist (96 staff-months), rural engineer/contracts expert (30 staff-
months), training (36 staff-months), financial management/accountancy
(120 staff-months), periodic rapid rural appraisal and evaluation and other
short-term studies (20 staff-months); and
(b)   some short-term assistance open to international recruitment for:
developing a training program (18 staff-months); regional
development/project management (6 staff-months); establishment of an
M&E system (6 staff-months); an initial baseline survey of income and
production information (12 staff-months); a mid-term review, and an
evaluation of development impact at project completion.
3.23  The findings and recommendations coming out of the mid-term review would be
issued in Bahasa Indonesia with a summary in English. Assurances were obtained from
GOI during negotiations that: (a) the baseline survey will be completed before March 31,
1997; and (b) the following consultants: civil works/contracts specialist, trainer, and
accountants would be appointed by August 31, 1996.
3.24 Operating Means and Supplies. The project provides for financing a vehicle per
each involved DINAS, 6 motorcycles per Kecamatan, office equipment and supplies for
field services of the Dinas, extension staff, community development, and supervisory staff.
Incremental recurrent costs including honoraria, staff allowances, operations and
maintenance costs, project coordination and management, at the national, provincial,
Kabupaten and kecamatan have also been included in the cost of the project and would be
financed by the Bank on a declining scale.
3.25  The Implementation Schedules for activities under the Capacity Strengthening and
Project Management Component are in Annex 7 Table 2 & 3.
Component C. Farming Systems and Fisheries Research (US$ 1.6 million; 5 percent
of total base costs).
3.26  This component aims at supporting and extending the on-farm trial program of the
two regional Assessment Institutes of Agricultural Technology (AIAT) established by
AARD in Biromaru and Kendari. The main activities of the AIATs, according to the new
orientation adopted by AARD for national agricultural research will be related to:
* regional problem identification through better research-extension-farmer
linkages;



23
*  closer collaboration between research and extension to generate and package
technologies to meet local farmers' needs;
*  technology transfer which would link researchers, extension workers and
farmers closer together;
*  organization of field days; and
*  production of materials and information which meet and recognize the
absorptive capacity and educational level of the farmers.
3.27  Most of the above activities will be carried out under the ARM II Project. The
financing will however be limited to a few sites and programs since available funding for
each AIAT under ARMP II will have to cover two provinces. In order to get a much
wider coverage of the project zones, financing will be provided under this component to
carry out various applied research programs, including post-harvest research, and fisheries
research in the two provinces, not covered under the research program being financed by
ARMP II. In addition to their own programs under ARMP II therefore, both AIATs will
support the SAAD project in certain areas of development in the two provinces. They will
support the project in three main areas:
(a) assist the Dinas-Dinas Food, Estate Crops, Livestock and Fisheries extension
agents in the transfer of proven agricultural technologies and in the design of
the most relevant agro-technological demonstrations on farmers fields as
funded under the project; The Dinas-Dinas extension agents will remain
responsible for the actual organization, lay-out and monitoring of the
demonstrations and organize farmer group discussion meetings. They will
ensure that women are involved in on-farm trials and field days, technology
testing and demonstrations, and other project support activities,
(b) carry out specific applied, priority research projects in support of regional
agricultural development and based on identified local specific needs; for this
the project provides 6 years of program funding; and
(c) establish, with the assistance of DGEC, new superior mother tree stock
gardens for cashew (Kendari) and cacao (Biromaru), 5 ha each, estimated at
RP 61 million/ garden.
3.28   One of the first AIAT activities programmed under the ARMP 2 is a Farming
Systems Research baseline survey to help identify the main applied research needs and to
help program farming systems including fisheries research and technology transfer
activities for the AIATs zones of coverage. In order to fully exploit the results of the FSR
survey, two extra man months for the FSR specialist would be paid for under the SAADP
to review the initial results of the baseline surveys in both provinces and organize two
regional research workshops to be attended by staff of the AIAT, the agricultural technical
agencies, the local planning agencies, and farmer representatives. The specific applied
priority research program in support of regional agricultural development mentioned in (b)
above will be determined at these workshops. Annex 2 gives details of the activities under



24
the applied research program and the Implementation Schedule for the component is in
Annex 7 Table 4.
Status of Preparation and Implementation Schedule
(a) 3.29    The project was prepared by the Directorate General for Regional

4. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING

expressed in October 1995 prices. Physical contingencies are estimated at 10 percent for
civil works, equipment and vehicles and 5 percent for agricultural inputs, breeding
animals, and research. Price contingencies are based on an estimated annual local inflation
rate of 8.7 percent and a foreign inflation rate of 2.4 percent from FY96. Project costs are
summarized in Table 4.1 below and detailed in Annex 4.

Financing Plan
4.2     The proposed Bank loan of US$26.8 million equivalent would finance 63 percent
of total project costs, or 69 percent of project cost exclusive of taxes, duties, vehicles and
fertilizers which would be purchased by GOI. The Governments (Central, provincial and
Kabupaten) would finance US$12.1 million equivalent (28.4 percent of project cost) and



26
the balance of US$3.7 million equivalent (8.6 percent of project cost) would be financed
by the beneficiaries contributing their labor for the demonstration and replication activities.
