Financing of Proposed Development Activities and Cost Recovery
4.3    The project would finance several crop development demonstration activities
covering irrigation, dryland farming, treecrop rehabilitation, and homegarden
improvement. All labor for demonstration activities and crop replication in the different
types of activities as well as labor for village infrastructure works will be supplied by
farmers. Annual inputs for irrigated cropping demonstrations, including farm tools,
improved seeds and fertilizers, would be supplied for two cropping cycles as grants to the
farmers participating in the demonstration programs. This subsidy is justified as these early
adopters would provide a demonstration effect for follower adopters.'3 There would be
one 2 ha demonstration per irrigation scheme with total area of demonstrations covering
about six percent of the targeted area of selected village irrigation schemes. Support will
also be provided for farmer seed growers to multiply improved seeds for sale to other
farmers, so as to initiate the introduction of improved varieties. Funds would be provided
through a village revolving credit scheme (para 4.6) for farmers who may want to replicate
the demonstration programs to purchase inputs. This one time grant to villages would
become a sustainable source of seasonal funding for farmers who would repay to their
communities at real and positive interest rates.
4.4    For dryland cropping the project would supply farm tools, fertilizers and other
required inputs for establishing demonstration farms and assistance for establishing tree
crop nurseries and for producing improved food crop seeds. Again the subsidy involved in
13 In economic language: these early adopters confer an extemality for follower adopters.



27
demonstration is justified due to the externality they generate for follower farmers. Those
who will want to replicate the dryland farming program may do so through the village
revolving credit scheme (para 4.6). There will be a one 3 ha demonstration farm for each 2
villages. Support for the demonstration and seed/seedling production programs will be for
two years. For houseplot cropping, arrangements would be similar as for dryland
cropping. There will be 2 demonstrations per village, each equivalent to about 0.2 ha.
Farmers' contribution will be in the form of labor for the demonstration activities.
4.5   For cattle raising the project would supply breeding stock to selected farmers
who: have developed or are already using sustainable farm practices; have not owned
livestock before; followed all the training; agree to develop the needed fencing and
stables; and have enough fodder crops planted to feed two animals year around. Priority
will also be given to farmers with a farm income below the average for the kecamatan.
Participating farmers would contract to return 2 heifers (1.5 years old) per cow received
(farmers who received a bull and a cow pay 3 heifers). Beneficiaries of the cattle
distribution program would return the second and third born calves for redistribution
among other interested farmers or for sale (usually the male calves) to cover part of the
operating cost of the livestock support services. An assurance to this effect was obtained
during negotiations. Management of the distribution and the cattle revolving credit
programs would be by the beneficiary communities (para 3.12 and Annex 1 Section G).
4.6   Inputs for Replication and Village-Level Revolving Credit. Though various
productivity improvement programs, financed by the Government, provide some means of
purchasing some agricultural inputs, direct farmer access to credit for input purchase is
often difficult. The commercial banking system has not shown much interest in managing
direct farmer credits and hence the project cannot rely on them to finance credits for
inputs. Project funds will be used to pay for inputs and even though the funds will be
passed on as a grant to the village farmers will be required to pay back the value of the
inputs, including interest, to the village to set up a village revolving credit. The credit will
not finance labor for replication since this is expected to be provided by the farmers.
Promising experiments are currently underway in Indonesia under the Yogyakarta Upland
Area Development Project (Loan 3305-IND) and the Integrated Swamps Development
Project (Loan 3755-IND) with farmer-managed revolving credit schemes. The proposed
project would therefore finance a similar scheme that would make funds available to
farmer groups for input purchase.
4.7   It is expected that community development facilitators (CDFs) and extension
agents (PPLs) would help farmers to organize themselves into farmer groups and help
them prepare proposals for replication of project-introduced technology that would be
reviewed by the appropriate Dinas for inclusion in the annual program and financing plan.
Terms of reference of the CDFs is in Annex I Appendix A. Funds will be a one-time grant
to participating villages, but farmer groups and individual farmers would have to repay the
credit with a minimum interest rate of 15 percent per year in the initial years and a higher
rate in the latter years. Individual communities will however be able to charge higher rates
if they wish. Management of the credit will be by a village credit committee that will



28
decide on credit terms, and ensure repayments. Details on operating conditions for the
credit were discussed at negotiations and are in Annex 1. Section I.
4.8   Each participating village would receive a revolving credit that would not exceed
Rp 10 million/year for distribution to farmer groups in the village. Repayments may be
given in priority to new farmers willing to adopt the package in the same village. Peer
pressure within the village setting is expected to lead to high rates of reimbursement as is
the case under the Hamlet Credit program of the Yogya Upland Agriculture Development
Project. In addition it is proposed that as part of the process of ensuring full repayment,
villages with farmer groups having overdue loans may not be provided further grants.
Over time, other farmers may either become members of already existing groups, or form
new groups. As experience with grassroots credit schemes under this and other projects
evolves, the training of facilitators would be updated and new formats would be
introduced. Assurance was obtained at negotiations that operation of the village grant and
revolving credit program would be under agreed conditions. Details on the operating
conditions under the village revolving credit scheme including regulations, operational
guidelines, and credit conditions for the revolving credit system (Annex 1 Section l) were
discussed and agreed during negotiations.
Procurement
4.9    General. Goods and works would be procured in accordance with the provisions
of the "Guidelines for the Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits", published by
the Bank in January 1995 and revised in January 1996. Procurement under the project
would be undertaken by the respective implementing units under the guidance and control
of provincial and/or national parent agencies. Bulking and grouping of contracts across
agencies or provinces would not be practical and has therefore not been adopted. GOI
would finance purchases of vehicles and fertilizer (urea, TSP. and KCL) from its own
resources because under GOI procurement rules these items are not open for international
competitive bidding. Farmers may however use funds from the grant component for
financing replication inputs to purchase fertilizers. This component will be financed at 70
percent to take account of the fact that the share of fertilizer in the inputs to be purchased
is about 30 percent to be financed by Government. Procurement of vehicles would be in
accordance with a schedule and timetable agreed with the Bank. An assurance to this
effect was obtained from GOI during negotiations
4.10  Civil Works. Contracts for the rehabilitation of agricultural extension centers,
seed farms, and animal health posts (US$0.7 million) are expected to be relatively small
and would be widely dispersed over the two provinces. The major part (US$0.6 million)
would therefore be procured by national competitive bidding (NCB) procedures
acceptable to the Bank. A small part of such contracts (US$0.1 million ) would involve
very minor works dispersed over time and space and may, with the Bank's prior
agreement, be procured on force account. Contracts for roads, bridges and culverts
(US$10.8 million) would normally be small, costing less than $50,000 each and would be
procured using simplified procurement procedures similar to national shopping. Under



29
this procedure, small works up to the aggregate amount of US$10.8 million would be
procured under lump-sum, fixed-price contracts awarded on the basis of quotations
obtained from three qualified domestic contractors in response to a written invitation. The
invitation would include a detailed description of the works, including basic specifications,
the required completion date, a basic form of agreement acceptable to the Bank, and
relevant drawings, where applicable. The award would be made to the contractor who
offers the lowest price quotation for the required work, and who has the experience and
resources to complete the contract successfully.
4.11   Equipment and Agricultural Inputs. Equipment and furniture for extension,
seed production and animal health facilities (US$0.2 million), most agricultural inputs in
the form of pesticides, tools, some bulk veterinary supplies (US$1 .1 million) would be
procured by NCB procedures acceptable to the Bank. The remaining agricultural inputs
(mainly planting material and some veterinary medicine) will be procured by national
shopping for packages costing less than US$50,000 equivalent, with an aggregate value
not to exceed US$1.0 million. For the procurement of breeding animals, it is expected
that a number of farmer groups would want to shop locally for their breeding animals
while others may want the assistance of the project to procure the animals. It is proposed
therefore that contracts costing less than US$30,000 up to a maximum amount of US$1.6
million may be acquired through national shopping. The other half of breeding animals
(US$1.5 million) will be procured by NCB procedures acceptable to the Bank. Grants will
be given to villages to purchase replication inputs and for agricultural marketing and
processing. Contracts for these will normally be entered into by individual farmer groups
in packages of less than $10,000 at a time, and therefore national shopping procedures will
be used for the purchase of replication inputs and for marketing and processing financed
under the grant component up to the aggregate amount of US$6.3 million.
4.12  Other Procurement. Consultant services (US$ 4.5 million), including contracts
to be signed with CDOs (US$1.9 million) would be contracted according to procedures
acceptable to the Bank. Separate contracts would be let by AARD for consultants
providing support to research (US$0.1 million) and by BANGDA and/or the provincial
BAPPEDAs for consultant services for strengthening of agricultural support services and
project administration (US$2.6 million). All consultants would be selected in accordance
with the Bank's Guidelines for the Use of Consultants, August 1981, and engaged
according to the Bank's Standard form of Contract for Consultant Services, June 1995.
Contracts for CDOs would be treated as consulting services except that it would be by
sole source selection with CDOs under conditions acceptable to the Bank (para 5.10).
Sole source selection of CDOs would be necessary since there are only very few CDOs
operating in the project provinces with the requisite experience, staffing and language
capability to provide the community development services being asked for. Training
(US$0.9 million), mapping (US$0.4 million), and research (US$1.1 million) would be
carried out in accordance with the above guidelines. Procurement for activities listed
under the community development sub-component (US$2.7 million), that have yet to be
identified, would in general follow national shopping procedures for goods and small
works contracts for civil works.

4.14 Review of Procurement. The Bank would make a prior review of all documents
related to the award of: (a) all contracts US$100,000 equivalent or more for civil works



31
and goods; (b) the first contract for civil works in Central and Southeast Sulawesi of less
than US$100,000; (c) the first contract for goods in Central and Southeast Sulawesi of
less than US$100,000; and (d) all contracts of US$100,000 equivalent or more for
consultants' services by firms and all contracts of US$50,000 equivalent or more for
consultants' services by individuals. Due to the repetitive nature of small contracts,
characteristic for this type of project, prior review is expected to cover only about 50
percent by value of all civil works construction and supply of goods contracts. For
consultant contracts, the review would include terms of reference, shortlist, letters of
invitation, recommendation for award, and final contract. The exceptions to prior Bank
review of contracts for consultants' services would not apply to: (a) the terms of reference
for such contracts; (b) single-source selection of consulting firms; (c) assignments of a
critical nature, as reasonably determined by the Bank; (d) amendments to contracts for the
employment of consulting firms raising the contract value to US$ 100,000 equivalent or
more; or (e) amendments to contracts for the employment of individual consultants raising
the contract value to US$50,000 equivalent or more. The Bank would review all other
procurement ex-post and at random.
Disbursements
4.15  All activities under the project are expected to be completed by December 31,
2002 and the Loan closing date would be June 30, 2003. Disbursements from the Loan
would be made for:
(a)    65 percent of expenditures on civil works;
(b)    100 percent of foreign expenditures (CIF) for directly imported equipment
and fjrniture, 100 percent of local expenditures (ex factory) for locally
manufactured items, and 65 percent of expenditures on locally procured
items;
(c)    90 percent of expenditures on consultant services, surveys and mapping,
training courses, and agricultural inputs (except fertilizers) for
demonstration and seed farms, breeding animals, and research trials and
demonstrations;
(d)   70 percent of expenditures for grants to villages for acquisition of
replication inputs and agricultural processing and marketing and for
services, works, and equipment procured under the "other local community
initiatives" sub-component; and
(e)    60 percent of incremental operating cost for expenditures incurred in
FY97 and FY98, 50 percent of expenditures incurred in FY99 and FY00,
and 40 percent of expenditures incurred in FY01 and thereafter.
Annex 5 Table 2 gives the proposed allocation of the Loan by Category.



32
4.16  Disbursements under the project would be made against Statement of
Expenditures (SOE) for:
(a)   contracts for works, goods (including agricultural inputs and breeding
animals) valued at less than US$100,000, except for the first contract for
goods and for works in Sulawesi Tengah and Sulawesi Tenggara,
(b)   contracts for the employment of consultants valued at less than
US$100,000 for firms and US$50,000 for individuals; and
(c)   expenditures for survey, design and mapping, replication inputs, training,
research and development expenditures, local community initiatives, and
incremental operating costs.
4.17  Disbursements from the Loan for the following contracts would be made against
full documentation: (a) works and goods contracts (including agricultural inputs, breeding
animals, and local community initiatives) and consultant services by firms valued at or
more than US$100,000; and consultant services contracts with individual consultants
valued at more than US$50,000. All other disbursements would be made against
statements of expenditures (SOE) for which relevant documents would be retained by
responsible project implementing agencies and the Directorate General of Budget
(Ministry of Finance) and be made available for inspection by Bank supervision mission.
Accounts and Audits
4.18  In order to facilitate disbursements, the Government would establish a Special
Account in the amount of US$2.5 million (equivalent to the average disbursements during
a four-month period) in Bank Indonesia. This account should be maintained by the
Directorate General of Budget, MOF. All disbursements from the Bank loan would be
made through the Special Account. Replenishment applications to the Special Account
will be made on a monthly basis, or when 20 percent of the SA balance has been used,
whichever comes first.
4.19  Each project executing agency would establish and maintain adequate and separate
accounts from the beginning of project implementation, including accounts relating to the
cattle distribution program. To facilitate audit of project accounts, BANGDA (the project
lead agency) would collect and consolidate account records of all implementing agencies
into a single document. Assurances were received from GOI during negotiations that: (a)
all project accounts, including schedules of expenditures claimed under SOE procedures
and usage of the Special Account, would be audited annually by the Government Audit
Agency (BPKP), or other suitably qualified auditors acceptable to the Bank; and (b) audit
reports of project accounts, SOE and Special Account would be furnished to the Bank
within nine months of the close of each Government fiscal year.



33
5. ORGANIZATION, IMPLEMENTATION AND
MONITORING
Organizational Structure
5.1   The proposed project would be implemented in a decentralized, multi-sectoral
manner with primary responsibility for project implementation at the Kabupaten and lower
levels, for purposes of ensuring ownership of the project of the local Governments and the
rural population. Organizational arrangements are shown in Chart 1. Project managers
would mostly be at the Kabupaten level (TK II). This decentralized project implementation
approach will ensure that the two principal project components; the agriculture-based area
development, and capacity strengthening, will be directly implemented by the local
governments.
5.2   At the national level, a Central Guidance committee (CGC) chaired by
BAPPENAS (Deputy V Regional Development) and including, among others,
representatives from the Ministries of Home Affairs (DG Regional Development, and
Director Area Development), Finance and Agriculture (DGFCH, DGLS, DGE, DG
Fisheries, and DG AARD) will be responsible for providing policy guidance and
coordination, for reviewing project progress, and resolving issues on overall Loan
administration. To provide support for the CGC and to coordinate project activities, a
small project secretariat will be set up at the Directorate of Area Development in
BANGDA. This unit is expected to maintain close liaison with the Directorate Generals of
Food Crops, Estate Crops, Livestock and Fisheries, all of the Ministry of Agriculture, to
ensure that appropriate technical support from the sector agencies can be given to the
provinces and Kabupatens when necessary. The project secretariat will ensure that the
above four DGs participate at least twice a year in project supervision. The main activities
of the unit will be:
(a)   to coordinate project planning and budgeting at the national and provincial
levels;
(b)   to review annual work plans and budgets and process the Decision Letter
for Budget Assistance (SPABP) for the provinces;
(c)   to monitor and evaluate project implementation and impact;
(d)   to prepare periodic project progress reports (annual/semi-annual);
(e)   to implement national level activities such as project orientation
workshops, coordination, recruitment of consultants, etc.; and
(f)   to liaise with the Bank, administer loan disbursements and consolidate
project accounts from the provinces.



34
5.3   At the provincial level, a Provincial Coordination Committee (PCC) chaired by
the BAPPEDA I Chairman would be responsible for coordination, planning, supervision,
monitoring, and technical support of project activities. The committee would include the
Agriculture Kanwil, and the Dinas-Dinas of agriculture, public works, as well as
community development. The provincial environmental bureau (BKLH) will also be part
of the committee to ensure that all project activities conform to acceptable environmental
standards. A project secretariat, headed by the chief of the Economic Division of
Bappeda, will be appointed to coordinate and implement any activities at the provincial
level. The functions of the secretariat would be similar to those of the national project
secretariat and would include:
(a)   ensuring province-wide coordination of all project activities;
(b)   reviewing for approval by the PCC, the Kabupaten activities, annual work
plans and budgets;
(c)   monitoring and evaluating implementation and preparing progress reports;
(d)   preparing loan disbursement applications for Components A and B;
(e)   carrying out provincial level activities; and
(f)   liaising with central Government agencies, Kabupaten, and the Bank.
5.4   For each of the Kabupaten (TK I) levels, a project coordinator will be appointed
at the Bappeda TK 11, with project managers (PIMPROs) appointed at each of the
participating Dinas-Dinas. . An assurance was obtained at negotiations that the
coordinators and project managers will be appointed by August 31, 1996 and will be
provided with competent staff in adequate numbers. The PIMPROs will be responsible
for the implementation of the field activities working with a team at the district level and
with Kecamatan level teams. The latter would constitute the basic unit for local level
planning, project implementation and organization of community participation.
5.5   Though the applied research component (C) is expected to be implemented by the
AIATs, consistent with arrangements adopted under the ARM II project, each AIAT
would establish a Regional Advisory Committee (RAC) and a Regional Technical
Working Group (RTWG) in each province to provide guidance for AIAT during project
implementation. Each RAC would be chaired by the head of each provincial BAPPEDA as
first chair with the head of MOAs regional coordinating office (Kanwil) as second chair.
Members of the RAC would be drawn from various Dinas-Dinas, local universities, research
institutes, farmers' groups, local CDOs and private sector. The RAC guides the AIAT and its
Director on the R&D program, priorities and policies. The RTWG would be chaired by the
AIAT Director with representation from various regional and provincial agencies. AARD



35
will designate officers at CASER and CARP (Center for Agricultural Research Program)
to liaise with the Project Secretariat and the Bank.
Implementation Arrangements
5.6   The approach for implementing the Agriculture Development Component has
been formulated to maximize the participation of the targeted beneficiaries in selecting,
planning and implementing the project interventions. Project activities would be planned
through the existing bottom-up planning system with field extension workers (FEW) and
community development facilitators (CDF) assisting rural communities in planning of
project activities. The facilitators, to be recruited on contract by the project, may be
recruited locally, from universities, or from among CDOs. They, together with the FEW
and other community development staff, would help farmers and farmer groups:
(a) to identify their main constraints and priority needs;
(b) to review the menu of activities and select the most appropriate option
provided by the project that is needed in their village based on the natural
conditions and comparative advantage of the area;
(c) to prepare a request and submit this request through the bottom-up planning
process for financing;
(d) to prepare and implement the agreed on activity including management of any
village level revolving credit that the group may ask for; and
(e) to monitor, evaluate and make mid-course corrections in project
implementation as necessary
5.7   The village level proposals would be reviewed and consolidated at the Kecamatan
level and retained proposals will be sent to the Kabupaten level. The Kabupaten project
staff will review all project proposals and prepare a draft annual operational plan and
budget for onward transmission to the provincial level steering committee for review and
adoption.
5.8   Apart from the options provided through the identified menu of activities, rural
communities and local CDOs would also be encouraged to identify other priorities, not
included in the core menu, for development in their communities and prepare proposals for
assistance under a community development fund included as part of the project. Under
this program, the CDFs and FEWs will assist farmers, farmer groups and/or village
communities to identify activities and make proposals for funding. The village level
proposals, formulated by farmer groups and/or villages with assistance from the community
development facilitators, will be consolidated at the Kecamatan level and reviewed for funding
as described in para 3.15 and Annex 1 Section H.



36
5.9   Given the limited capacity existing at the local levels to manage and teach
community-based development, it is proposed that the project finance community
development facilitators to strengthen the current local government structure. A special
effort would be made to work with institutions familiar with participatory development
and to train local officials in community development approaches. In addition during the
early years of the proposed project, assistance would be provided to strengthen local
capacity to carry out this approach through short-term training in community based
planning for community leaders and technical agencies at the kecamatan and village levels.
5.10  During negotiations, the GOI Delegation expressed the view that some effort
should be made in the project to foster the growth of grassroots community development
organizations (CDOs). The Delegation however indicated that in the two provinces, there
may not be grassroots CDOs that currently have the personnel or experience to be able to
provide the entire services required in the project over a large geographical area. It was
therefore agreed that more experienced CDOs (national or international) should be paired
up with local CDOs to ensure that the local organizations get the exposure, training and
management assistance that they require in providing support to the village and farmer
groups.
5.11  Procedures and Criteria for Selecting CDOs. It was agreed during negotiations
that, to the extent possible, one local CDO will be selected per Kabupaten while a more
experienced CDO will be selected per province to enter into partnership with those
selected per Kabupaten (the same experienced CDO can however be selected for the two
provinces). In accordance with the requirements under the Bank's Consultant Guidelines,
the following criteria and procedures for selection were agreed:
*  the experienced CDO together with the local CDO will agree on the community
development facilitators (CDF) for each Kabupaten (terms of reference set out in
Attachment 3);
*  there will be I or 2 CDFs per Kecamatan depending on the number of villages;
*  the CDOs will provide a community development coordinator (CDC - terms of
reference in Attachment 3) and a gender/youth coordinator at the Kabupaten level;
*  the involvement of CDOs will be by invitation;
*  for each Kabupaten, the Bappeda Tk II will invite proposals from at least three local
CDOs that can work in the Kabupaten. These CDOs will make proposals on how they
will provide support to the community development program of the project. For the
CDO at the provincial level, the Bappeda Tk I will invite proposals from two or three
national or international CDOs with experience in the province. The form of the
proposals is included in Attachment 3;
*  Bappeda Tk II will review the proposals and select the CDO providing the best
proposal for the Kabupaten. Bappeda Tk I will review the selection to ensure that the
selected CDO meets the agreed selection criteria;
*  the selection of the provincial level CDO will be made by Bappeda Tk I based on the
review of the proposals and after discussion with Bappeda Tk II; and



37
the proposed CDO team for each province will be forwarded to the project secretariat
at the national level and to the Bank, with the criteria used for their selection.
5.12 For the Project Management and Capacity Strengthening Component, all
training for field, sub-district, district staff would take place in the target area or in the
Kabupaten or Kecamatan headquarters and would be organized by the project trainer in
consultation with the concerned staff. Arrangements for the acquisition of vehicles and
equipment will be made at the provincial level to permit regrouping.
5.13  For the Research Component, annual research plans and on-farm demonstration
programs would be prepared following procedures set up for the AIATs as part of the
ARMP II and as described in Annex 2. Each annual research plan would be reviewed by
the Technical Working Groups and approved by the Advisory Committees. The
approved work plan and budgets for the two provinces would be incorporated in the work
program for each province and furnised to CARP through CASER for DIP counterpart
funding. Trials and demonstrations funded under the proposed project would be carried
out in farmers' fields. AIAT staff would, together with Dinas staff, would design and
select the sites for all trials and demonstrations and would ensure adequate coverage of all
project areas. Field staff of the respective Dinas TK. II and village extension staff on site
would ensure the day-to-day management of the trials and demonstrations.
Budget Procedures
5.14  Project activities would be financed in accordance with existing GOI procedures
for financing Bank-assisted projects. Counterpart funds for project activities would be
provided under the budget of the Ministry of Agriculture for Component C. Funds for
Components B and C would be from "On-Top" funds financed through the budget of
the Ministry of Home Affairs and from the provincial and Kabupaten development budgets
(APBD TK I and TK II). Budget allocations for project activities would be based on
consolidated annual work programs agreed among project implementation agencies and
BAPPEDA at the provincial level. The budgeting process under the proposed project
would comprise the following steps:
(a)   the provincial BAPPEDAs would review the work programs proposed at
local levels and as described in para 3.15 and prepare consolidated
provincial work plans and draft budgets;
(b)   the provincial work plans and draft budgets would be sent to the National
Project Secretariat for review and consolidation and thereafter submitted to
BAPPENAS, AARD, MoHA and the provincial and Kabupaten authorities
in August of each year for incorporation in their annual budget proposals;
(c)   the consolidated work plans and cost estimates would be forwarded to the
Bank by October 31 of each year for review; and



38
(d)   the final budget proposals based on the agreed work plan would be
forwarded to the Bank before December 31 of each project year.
5.15  To ensure that adequate funds would be provided for planned project activities,
assurances were obtained from GOI at negotiations that GOI would furnish to the Bank
for its review and comments: (a) not later than October 31 each year, a consolidated work
program and cost estimates for the following fiscal year; and (b) by December 31 each
year, coordinated budget proposals for the provincial work programs agreed with the
Bank.
Monitoring and Evaluation and Performance Indicators
5.16  Given the scale of community-based activities and the decentralized project
management structure proposed for the project, close monitoring will be an essential
feature of the project to permit continuous evaluation of project progress and to propose
measures to ensure effective and efficient implementation. The M&E system would be
similar to that designed for the proposed Nusa Tenggara Area-based Agriculture
Development Project (NTAADP) and would include impact analysis for the main
agricultural activities: village irrigation, dryland farming, livestock distribution, and other
local community initiatives, in addition to the regular reports on financial and physical
progress. Participation of women and youth in the various project activities will be
monitored in the M&E program.
5.17  The first level of monitoring reports would be from Kabupaten level PIMNPROs to
the Provincial Project Secretariat. These reports would be prepared quarterly and used to
respond immediately to any problems encountered in implementing the project activity and
also to elicit any technical help that may be necessary from the higher level technical
agencies (provincial or central). The quarterly reports would be discussed with Bank
Supervision missions. The second level of monitoring would be on the physical,
environmental and financial progress of the project through mandated reporting forms
from the implementing agencies varying according to the source of funding but updated at
least every semester. The third level would seek to provide simple quantitative feedback
on the performance of the project's major activities through impact indicators collected
regularly and updated yearly to provide a data base for evaluating the financial, economic
and social impact of project interventions on target beneficiaries. The fourth and final
method would be rapid appraisal monitoring. This method is recommended as: (a) a
means of direct interaction with the target population; and (b) a useful management tool to
achieve coordination among the implementing agencies. Rapid appraisal should be
conducted once a year at the Kabupaten level with representation from the Kabupaten
Dinas, kecamatan staff, participating CDOs and the provincial BAPPEDA office. A
consultant will be financed in each of the two provinces to set up and manage the
monitoring process.
5.18  The proposed evaluation system has been designed to evaluate project outcomes in
relation to the project objectives. A three tiered system would be used consisting of:



39
(a)   an initial inventory or data base on income and production information at
the provincial and Kabupaten levels, building on the data gathered during
project preparation;
(b)   a mid-term review after two years of project life to review progress,
identify management and technical constraints, assess development impact
and recommend adjustments in project design, as appropriate; and
(c)   a final review at project completion to evaluate the direct impact of the
project on target groups and the effectiveness of the organizational
structure and management system used in the project, and to draw
conclusions for future projects.
5.19  Details on the proposed project performance indicators are given in Table 5.1. This
is a comprehensive list discussed during negotiations which Government agreed to
monitor. It was however agreed that a few key indicators from the above list will be
selected during early supervision for close monitoring though Government indicated that
given the new approach in this project, it would like to monitor as many f the indicators as
possible.
Bank Supervision
5.20  As the project is geographically dispersed and involves a novel approach to
providing services to the rural population, the Bank should schedule supervision missions
initially at least twice a year, starting off with two main project launch workshops in the
two provinces and mini workshops in each Kabupaten. In general, the fall supervision
missions will concentrate on the work programs and budgets for the following years and
the spring missions on the results achieved in the field during the preceding rainy season.
A mid-term review is also programmed for early 1999 even though each supervision
mission would review project progress and consider necessary adjustments. An assurance
was obtained from GOI during negotiations that a mid-term review report would be
furnished to the Bank by March 31, 1999, for review and discussion with GOI by May 31,
1999. The Bank would rely to the extent possible on the Bank's Resident Mission staff
(RSI) to interact continuously with the central and local project agencies and to undertake
quick field visits to provide implementation assistance as necessary. A supervision plan
for the project is at Annex 7. It details the mix of specialties and frequency and duration
of missions needed to ensure adequate supervision by the Bank. In addition, RSI would
conduct ex-post reviews of works completed and goods supplied at the rate of about two
staff-weeks per year.

6. PROJECT BENEFITS, AGRICULTURAL
PRODUCTION, MARKETING AND FARM INCOMES
Project Benefits
6.1   The project would increase smallholder productivity and incomes, and raise the
capacity of the local government agencies and village institutions to provide agricultural
services to farmers and to promote community participation in development. The project,
together with others, would also support and supplement GOI's ongoing efforts to
improve the standards of living in the country's eastern islands and thus reduce regional
income disparities. The main benefits are categorized below (see Annex -, Table - for
further details):
(a) direct impact on target beneficiaries through increased agricultural
production and incomes, support for farming systems research, and improved
rural infrastructure, especially roads. Because of the dense network of
demonstration activities to be financed under the project, at least over 80
percent of the rural population (180,000 households) would be exposed to the
project's activities. Cumulatively, over 100,000 households (or about 45
percent of the households) would directly benefit from project financing for
either demonstration or replication of one or the other of the interventions
under Component A (irrigation, dryland farming, treecrop rehabilitation, cattle
distribution, and houseplot improvements, chicken vaccination, community
initiatives, etc.). The village revolving credit program as well the introduction
of better production technology generated by research (Component C),
improvement of agricultural support services, and redistribution of breeding
cattle, would further increase the number of beneficiaries beyond those
estimated above, after the project ends; and
(b) improved capacity of local planning and technical agencies to plan,
appraise, monitor and execute multi-sectoral development programs and to
correct program implementation whether the program is locally or externally
financed. Civil servants and local government staff from the two provinces
would receive short-term training and gain practical experience from the
project to plan better for future development, improve future utilization of
development resources and replicate the experience of the project in other
areas.
Agricultural Production
6.2   The impact of project activities on agricultural production has been analyzed based
on different farm models that represent the farming typologies of the two provinces. Two
farm model typologies were used for irrigated production, three for dryland farming, three
for house gardening and two for treecrop rehabilitation.



45
6.3    Farm Models Analyses. The impact of the project on production in irrigated
agriculture have been based on two farm model types representing some 20,300 farms, at
full development, with an average size of 0.5 ha irrigated land on some 203 farmer-
managed village irrigation systems. For dryland agriculture, estimates are based on three
farm model types representing some 52,000 farms at full development (PYIO) with an
average size of 0.75 ha rainfed land. It has further been assumed that some 10 percent of
the irrigated farms and up to 50 percent of the dryland farms would take up livestock
keeping under the project by acquiring breeding cattle. Also, three farm model types
covering some 8,800 farm households were reviewed to analyze the expected to increase
in the production from home gardens.
6.4    Though assumptions underlying the farm models have been made as realistic as
possible, the selected models are obviously simplifications of diverse situations existing in
the field. Soil and water conditions in the project areas and cultural preferences of the
people are the determinant factors in the choice of farming systems. The selected models
obscure large variations in climatic conditions and productive potentials in the two
provinces, between different areas of the same province and within islands. Also, there
will be large differences in the time periods farmers will need to adopt improved
cultivation practices, in some cases continuing beyond the project horizon and
necessitating the making of assumptions for the purpose of quantifying project benefits.
The models however are derived from current cultivation practices in Southeast and
Central Sulawesi and capture the essentials of the prospects for improving agriculture in
the two provinces. Generally, a conservative stance has been maintained for the
projections of future production. Though full development is not expected until PYIO in
terms of areas replicated (adopted), we leveled off adoption in terms of area at PY7 for
purposes of our economic analysis.
6.5   Improvements in Productivity. Yield increases are assumed to derive from
farmers adopting improved water management and improved technology packages in the
form of higher yielding seed varieties, modern inputs and better cultural practices. The
following assumptions were made regarding productivity improvements under the project:
(a) In the village irrigation program the project will not finance any physical
improvements to the schemes (diversion weirs, field canals, etc.) since these are
already being made under GOI's ongoing Village Irrigation Improvement program
(PID). The latter program however will not finance any agricultural improvements on
completed schemes. The village irrigation model used here therefore assumes that the
present project would follow up on the infrastructure improvements with field
demonstrations and extension of improved production practices (HYV seeds,
fertilizers, etc.), organization of farmer and water user groups, plus financing (through
the village credit scheme) for replication. The project interventions are expected to
result in additional increases in the yields of the two main crops paddy (from an
estimated average 4.0 tons to 5.5 ton/ha) and soybeans (from 0.8 tons to 1.2 tons/ha).



46
Cropping intensity is assumed to increase from 1.0 to 1.10. A Table on the rate of
adoption of the technology is in Annex 1 Table 1.
(b) In the dryland production program, the crops currently produced (without project)
are upland rice and beans (mungbeans, soybeans, etc.) with a cropping intensity of
about 91 percent. The technology to be introduced under the project would permit
farmers to diversify into tree crops (mainly cashew, fruit trees), fodder/fuel trees, and
grass. Since the technology aims at helping farmers intensify their production and
become sedentary, the upland rice would be replaced in the rotation by maize. They
would also be encouraged to intensify food crop cultivation. Three farm model types
have been used (Annex I Table A3) and these models assumes an increase in average
cropping intensity to about 141 percent. This increase would occur gradually,
allowing sufficient time for farmers to test the recommended new practices. Maize
yields would increase from 1.5 to about 2.0 to 2.2 tons/ha for farmers who would
adopt the application of basic fertilizer dosages (Models 2 & 3) but only about 1.6
ton/ha for those who would not (Model 1). Mungbean yields would increase from 600
to 700 kg (Model 1) or to 900 kg/ha (Models 2 &3). Cashew trees would start
yielding 1 kg dry raw nuts/tree from year 6, increasing to about 7.5 kg nuts/tree by
year 11 (Model 2); the average farmer would have about 3 3 trees. A Table on the rate
of adoption of the dryland technology is in Annex 1 Table 2.
(c) In the Treecrop Rehabilitation program, there would be two basic types of activities:
rehabilitation of existing overgrown cashew (in general not shaded); and rehabilitation
of cacao farms (shaded by coconut or other trees or bananas). For cashew, it is
recommended to reduce the number of mature trees to some 33 trees/ha and plant
foodcrops in between, such as corn and mungbeans. Alternatively when high quality
cashew seedlings are available (about Year 5 or 6 of project), they could be planted to
replace the ones which have been cut out, which in turn will replace the oldest trees
after some 4 to 6 years. For purposes of our farm model analyses however, we have
used only the first approach (Annex I Table A4). For the cacao model (Annex 1
Table A5), it is recommended to apply proper pruning techniques to improve tree
shapes, fruit bearing, crop sanitation and ventilation and improve the quality of the
shade trees by planting lamtoro and gamal trees. For cacao, yields are estimated to
increase from 350 kg/ha in the without project situation to about 800kg/ha five years
after rehabilitation (starting from 250kg) and for cashew, yields are expected to go up
from 350 kg/ha to 700 kg/ha in Year 5 (starting from some 200kg/ha). In addition
the inter-cropped maize is expected to yield the equivalent of 1.8 tons/ha and
mungbeans about 700 kg/ha. A Table on the rate of adoption of the treecrop
rehabilitation technology is in Annex 1 Table 3
(d) The house garden model (Annex 1 Table A6) assumes that those adopting the
program would replace cassava cultivation with some beans/vegetables and also plant
mangoes, jackfruit, vanilla, ginger and lamtoro. Modest increases in food crops (maize
and beans) and treecrop production are expected from use of manure, cash inputs and
better cultivation practices.



47
(e) For farmers receiving livestock, improved cattle feeding practices and better health
care are expected to (a) increase cattle birth rates from about 70 to 80 percent, and
(b) decrease mortality rates from 15 percent to 8 percent for animals under one year of
age and from 3 percent to 2 percent for animals over three years. For the poultry
vaccination program, in order to eliminate the problem posed by vaccines requiring
refrigeration, the project is to introduce a new technology of a heat resistant vaccine
(the V4 strain), now available in Indonesia. A community-based system relying on the
existing women network organizations, PKK, would be used to support the
vaccination program to control Newcastle disease among village chickens.
6.6   Incremental Production. Applying the assumptions in the farm model typologies
above to areas that can be covered in the project under proposals for demonstration and
replication activities, would lead to a direct project induced incremental annual production
at full development, equivalent to about 8,800 tons of paddy, 41,300 tons of maize,
12,300 tons of beans (soybeans and mungbeans), 2,960 tons of raw cashew nuts, 5,600
tons of cacao, about 1,000 tons of fruits and smaller production of vanilla and ginger.
The cattle distribution program and associated investments in animal health are expected
to add annually about 1,260 head of cattle (year 10). In practice the composition of
incremental production is likely to be far more diverse since farmers are essentially free to
cultivate what crops they want and for which there is demand. Crops that are likely to be
cultivated include coffee, different types of beans, more soybeans and fruit trees than
anticipated, and groundnuts. The value of the incremental production at full development
would be equivalent to over US$22.5 million per year (Annex 1 Table Al).
Farm Incomes.
6.7   Table 6.1 gives a summary of the expected farm incomes from the farm models
analyzed. Net farm incomes are expected to increase as a result of improvements to the
irrigated and dryland cropping systems, the treecrop rehabilitation program, cattle and
poultry keeping, and intensified home gardening - either separately or in combination. In
general therefore farmers adopting the proposed interventions are expected to obtain
substantial increases in net incomes, from their combined farming activities.
