6.8    Farm incomes for three activity models are given in Table 6.1. The models show,
among others, that net income from 0.5 ha irrigated land would increase by about 50
percent over the without project situation to about US$508 equivalent when pre-project
infrastructure improvements are followed by better agricultural practices. Net incomes on
an 0.75 ha dryland farm are expected to increase substantially from US$135 to the
equivalent of an US$280 at full development (year 10) with the recommended intensive
cultivation of food and tree crops, and to about US$210 equivalent if farmers would
continue to use only modest quantities of fertilizers on their food crops. Under the
rehabilitation program, farmers can expect to double their net incomes from the equivalent
of US$230 to about US$470 by adopting the recommended package. Net income per
workday would increase by about 18 percent on irrigated and by about 10 to 50 percent
on dryland farms. Similarly incomes from home-gardens are expected to double from
about US$36 equivalent on a plot of 0.2 ha to over US$82 through the introduction of
high value crops such as fruit trees, vanilla and spices. Further details are given in Annex
1, Tables Al to A4
6.9    Farmers who keep cattle, in addition to practicing improved irrigated or dryland
cropping, would be able to supplement their net incomes. However, during the early years
cash returns from a project-supplied heifer would be irregular and far apart. Under the
project, farmers would be required to return the first calf to the project, keep the second
and return the third. The second calf would be born about three to four years after the
arrival of the original heifer and, if sold, would be worth about US$150 equivalent one
year later. Only from the sixth or seventh year onwards would the cattle keepers be able
to regularly sell (once per year with some luck) descendants from the original heifer or her
retained daughter(s). Animal keepers other than those receiving breeding stock from the
project also would benefit because of the improvements to the animal health facilities,
resulting in lower mortalities and higher calving rates.



49
7. ECONOMIC JUSTIFICATION, IMPACT
ASSESSMENTS AND RISKS
Project Alternatives
7.1   Several alternative approaches to project design were considered during project
preparation, with the following being the key ones:
(a) Sector/centrally managed project. One main alternative approach to project
design would normally be a project designed centrally with components in the
provinces. In general however, these central-based sector projects have not
attempted to design operations to fit local constraints, but rather project areas
have been selected to fit project design. Such a central approach, for small
resource poor and remote provinces, such as those in the proposed project,
often leads to a general lack of balance in investments either between sectors
or between regions and little integration in programs. The regional approach
adopted here has allowed for a better local focus and review of the technical,
institutional, and incentive constraints of the project area and a project design
adapted to each area's conditions
(b) Regional coverage. Government's initial objective was to prepare one regional
development project covering at least seven provinces in the eastern islands
that it considered the poorest (Southeast and Central Sulawesi, East and West
Nusa Tenggara, East Timor, Maluku and Irian Jaya). In order to adapt project
design to specific regions and keep implementation and supervision
arrangements simple, the Bank and GOI eventually agreed to prepare separate
projects for the different areas. The Bank agreed to assist Government prepare
three of these projects covering one project for the two Nusa Tenggara
provinces, one for Maluku and the third for Central and Southeast Sulawesi.
Other donors (ADB and AUSAID) are helping Government with activities in
Irian Jaya and East Timor.
(c) Sector Coverage. Regional authorities were interested, during preparation, in
including activities from non-agricultural sectors. The Bank agreed with
Government that though other sector activities are important, implementation
experience of integrated rural development activities financed by the Bank has
shown that such projects are overly complex to manage and their poor
performance has been associated with project complexity and lack of flexibility.
The project scope was therefore limited to agriculture and associated
infrastructure development (roads), excluding sectors such as health care and
education. It was stressed however that though the project focus is on
agriculture, it is expected to complement other alternative non-agricultural
activities being financed by Government (e.g. through IDT), other donors



50
(Bank in Kabupaten Roads, Water Supply, Education, urban infrastructure,
etc.) and private individuals in trade and processing,
(d) Project Components. Several proposed sub-components for agricultural area
development were dropped for various reasons. Land titling was not included
because on balance comprehensive national coverage reflecting national
priorities is preferred over fragmented regional coverage. Goat distribution
was dropped as a sub-component because a large number of IDT village
groups are already involved in goat distribution. Though construction of
village irrigation structures was included during preparation, it was eventually
deleted because Government had just started a nationwide Village Irrigation
Schemes Project (PID). The main fisheries activities that were being requested
during preparation were additional fishing boats and outboard motors. This
was however excluded because the Eastern Islands study"4 showed that
increasing capitalization may be leading to rising and an unsustainable level of
indebtedness of coastal fishing communities. Similarly earlier projects
involving boat and outboard motor credits were not very successful and hence
these were excluded.
(e) Implementation Arrangements. The experience of NTASP and other donor-
financed projects that directly involved the communities, has confirmed the
importance of beneficiary participation in project implementation. Under the
proposed project therefore, the kabupaten and lower level technical agencies
would work closely together with area CDOs to organize farmers and further
encourage beneficiary participation in the planning and implementation of all
area-based schemes.
Fiscal Implications and Cost Recovery
7.2   The agriculture development component that makes up over 80 percent of project
cost would be implemented by the existing provincial and kabupaten technical services.
The project would support the Dinas by training their staff, providing them with transport
facilities and equipment, and paying allowances to facilitate effective implementation of all
sub-components. None of these support measures would have fiscal implications beyond
the project implementation period. There would be no recruitment of new staff except
perhaps those to be contracted by CDOs as additional community development
facilitators.
7.3   Since a few years ago, the Ministry of Agriculture transferred the operation and
maintenance of the Rural Extension Centers (REC) and Animal Health Centers
(Poskeswan) to the kabupaten governments. However these governments have not
provided adequate financing for their operation. One of the objectives of the project
14 Eastern Islands Rural Sector Strategy Review, Yellow Cover Report, November 1994, Chap 5



51
would be to make the existing RECs operational and convert them into multi-purpose
extension facilities and training and meeting center for the field level extension agents,
PPLs. A recent national study on decentralized study has confirmed that this is the
approach of follow. Similarly the project would work to make the Poskeswans effective.
For both the RECs and Poskeswans therefore, additional funding beyond what is currently
provided will be required to make them operational and effective. The project would
finance rehabilitation of the RECs and Poskeswans on condition that management plans
would be prepared and implemented for them (para. 3.16) including agreement from the
kabupaten concerned to pay the O&M costs of the facilities. Assurances to this effect
were obtained during negotiations. In addition, assurances were obtained that fees would
be charged to the users of the Poskeswan that would contribute to paying for the
operating costs of the centers.
7.4   Farmers would be asked to contribute to the implementation of sub-components,
such as roads and water infrastructure, usually in the form of labor. For the irrigated
cropping, dryland cropping, treecrop rehabilitation, and homeplot improvements sub-
components, the project provides for a revolving credit scheme (para ...) that would
finance inputs for adopters. The research and extension components are by and large
public goods and are not practically amenable to direct cost recovery.
Poverty Impact
7.5    A major emphasis of the Eastem Islands Study was on identifying policies and
activities that not only will promote agricultural growth but help reduce poverty in eastern
Indonesia. The proposed project is one such activity that has been identified as a result of
the recommendations of the sector study. One of the main criteria for selecting project
areas and activities was to reach a large number of households below or near the poverty
line and hence implicit in the objectives of the project is poverty alleviation. Thus the main
Kecamatans with high incidence of poverty were targeted for project intervention.
Incomes of the population in the target areas are generally low and hence the expected
increases in household incomes demonstrated in the farm budget analyses should
contribute to reduce poverty in these areas. Thus if estimates of the proposed project's
quantifiable benefits for households, which show doubling of incomes for dryland and
treecrop rehabilitation (US$135 to about US$280, and US$230 to about US$470
equivalent respectively) and a 50 percent increase for households doing irrigated cropping,
are realized then the impact of the project on poverty reduction should be significant. The
project is also likely to generate substantial, but as yet not quantifiable, benefits for the
people in the targeted areas, such as better road access and improved availability of farm
produce and farm inputs.
Economic Analysis
7.6   Treatment of Components. The project's Economic Rate of Return (ERR) was
estimated taking into account all project costs. Though research costs were added, no
direct benefits were assigned to the component. The impact of research would be long-



52
term in nature and would extend well beyond the project areas to the entire territories of
the two Sulawesi provinces. Although calculating an ERR for the research component
would be difficult, it would be possible to relate research cost to the size of the
agricultural sector in the two provinces to estimate an ERR. The project's research
component would cost about US$1.6 million over six years. This investment would
generate an economic rate of return of more than 50 percent if it were to prompt a one-
time 0.25 percent increase in agricultural production (a conservative assumption) in the
two provinces from the third project year onwards.
7.7    No attempts were made to estimate separate ERRs for individual project
components or for different productive sub-components because: (a) most components
and sub-components are complementary, for example cropping and cattle keeping; and (b)
there are no clear-cut ways to allocate the cost of supporting investments, e.g. road
improvements, technical assistance and training, among the separate productive activities.
The cost of "other local community initiatives" have been included in the calculations even
though those activities and their benefits have not been identified as yet.
7.8   The economic prices for fertilizers were based on prices derived from World Bank
international price projections. The economic prices (1995 constant) used for rice, maize,
soybeans, and fertilizer were based on parity prices derived from World Bank international
market projections for these commodities, adjusted for east Indonesia transport costs. The
economic price of cashew was based on a recent FAO study which projected economic
farm gate prices for this commodity in the same provinces. The economic prices of
mungbeans and livestock were equated with local market prices as production of these
commodities in the target areas is not import substituting. No discount factor for labor
costs was used as most project areas are not densely populated and economic wage levels
are assumed to equal local market wage rates of Rp. 2,500 per day. A 20 year planning
horizon was used in the analysis.
7.9    Rate of Return. The ERR of the project has been estimated at 21 percent. A
summary of the cost and benefit streams are at Annex 6, Table 2.
7.10   Sensitivity Analysis. If some of the risks alluded to in para. 7.18 were to
materialize, the effect on the project's ERR would be as indicated below:
(a)    delay of benefits (but not of costs) due to institutional or technical
implementation problems:
(i)    by I year: ERR 16 percent; and
(ii)    by 2 years: ERR 9.7 percent
(b)   decrease in gross benefit levels due to overestimation of local governments'
capacity to provide adequate project follow-up support, or because of



53
climatic or other technical difficulties (but all costs, including production
costs remaining as in base case):
(i)    by 10 percent : ERR 15.6 percent; and
(ii)    by 20 percent: ERR 9.1 percent
(c)   adoption levels for the crop development component lower than
anticipated due to farmers unwillingness to adopt the recommended new
technology (demonstration program however remains unchanged):
(i)   lower by fifty percent: ERR 13.6 percent; and
(ii)   lower by 75 percent for dryland and rehabilitation/50 percent
others: ERR 10.2 percent
7.11   Another sensitivity test conducted concerns the price of labor. Since there are
sometimes labor shortages during peak agricultural periods, we tested the ERR for the
possibility that the higher level of agricultural activities could lead to an increase in the
opportunity cost of labor. If project activities lead to an increase in the opportunity cost
of labor from the current Rp 2,500 per day to Rp 3,500 per day the project's ERR would
fall from 21 percent to 17.0 percent.
Environmental Impact Assessment
7.12   Environmental Screening. Proposed project interventions were screened during
preparation and an environmental analysis done of the different types of activities to assess
whether the proposed activities individually or cumulatively would result in any significant
impact on the environment and what environmental management procedures to put in
place. The main conclusions of the assessment are summarized below.
7.13   The assessment reviewed the environmental laws and procedures existing in
Indonesia and concluded that the project represents a category of activity that does not
clearly conform to any of the screening profiles in Indonesian environmental law. This is
because the project is multi-sectoral, and is designed to be implemented at the district level
in the form of numerous small, dispersed activities. In order to assess how project
activities fit into the environmental framework, the assessment sought to answer three
major questions: (i) whether any of the proposed categories of activities individually or
cumulatively result in significant impacts?; (ii) if the project taken as a whole would
produce significant cumulative impacts on the region?; and (iii) who is responsible for the
environmental management program under SAADP?
7.14  In trying to answer the above questions individual project activities were evaluated
to determine if they will produce significant impacts. The following characteristics of the



54
project area and the project design were reviewed and considered relevant to the
management of potential adverse environmental effects of the project:
(a) Physical Environment - Both provinces, but especially Central Sulawesi are
mountainous, with flat, arable land at a premium despite low overall population
density. The mountains are geologically young, with steep slopes, and highly erodible
soils in many areas. Settlements are clustered near the coast and in narrow inter-
montane valleys. Both provinces are predominated by infertile soils overlying
limestone and ultra-basic rocks. Southeast Sulawesi does contain large areas of
alluvial and rolling plains that could be made more productive, but rainfall is limited in
these areas".
(b) Land Classification/Protected Areas - Large portions of both provinces are within the
national forest estate, and a fair percentage of the forest land is classified in restrictive
use types such as Protection Forest or various types of natural area reserves The
island of Sulawesi is very biodiverse with high levels of endemism because it forms a
transition zone between the Asian and Australian bio-genetic realms. Central Sulawesi
contains three large and important protected areas: Lore Lindu National Park,
Morowali Strict Nature Reserve, and Togian Islands Strict Nature Reserve. Both
provinces contain a number of smaller protected areas
(c) Roads - Access to many of the areas in which project activities will be implemented is
difficult due to the poorly developed road network, making screening and monitoring
more difficult.
(d) Social - Rural populations are in general more isolated than in other provinces, and
have not been exposed to some of the production technologies being proposed under
the agriculture development component (A). Project participants will therefore
require greater than normal assistance to implement these activities in a technically and
environmentally sound manner. Community institutions are weak in many villages
because they are composed of relatively recent migrants from elsewhere in Sulawesi or
other Indonesian islands.; and
(e) Institutions - District and provincial government agencies in the two provinces are in
general underdeveloped compared with their counterparts in western Indonesia, and
have not had much experience implementing large projects or managing the
environmental
Environmental Impacts
7.15   The proposed activities do not pose an inherent threat to the environment,
particularly if implemented at low intensity levels and in widely scattered locations. Some
'5  The Ministry of Transmigration found as of 1988 that very few areas remain on the island of Sulawesi that have
not been settled and hence could be developed on a large scale basis (RePPProT).



55
of the activities could produce significant local impacts if sited or implemented improperly.
During appraisal Government decided that the villages and sub-districts adjacent to the
Lore Lindu National Park (Lore Utara, Lore Selatan, Kulawi and the southern part of
Biromaru) that had initially been considered for inclusion in the project, be excluded since
a proposed integrated area development project to be financed by the Asian Development
Bank would cover these areas. None of the other sub-districts or villages bordering the
main national parks either in Southeast or Central Sulawesi is in the project area and hence
project activities would have practically no impact on the Parks and Nature Reserves. The
main project activities are analyzed below in terms of their inherent potential to produce
impacts.
7.16   Crop Development Program. The various activities in this component would
include:
(a) Irrigated Cropping Technology: This activity involves providing training, tools, and
inputs to help farmers increase rice, soy bean and mungbean yields from existing
irrigated fields. The proposed project's intervention in the schemes is limited to
demonstrations of improved cropping and livestock practices, including assistance for
production and use of improved seeds. It does not involve the physical upgrading of
irrigation facilities which is being done before the start of the proposed project, or will be
improved simultaneously under Government's village irrigation scheme. Potential
adverse environmental impacts could result from improper handling or application of
pesticides or fertilizers resulting in water contamination on-site and off-site, or health
risks to farmers applying the chemicals. The potential adverse environmental impacts
related to improper handling and application of pesticides and fertilizers will be
mitigated by appropriate training of extension agents and by insuring that the agents
pass on this information to farmers. In association with the Directorate General of
Foodcrops integrated pest management techniques would be introduced.
(b) Dryland Farming Systems Development: Improved techniques for dryland cropping
on sloping land can have the positive environmental effects of conserving soil and
water while producing a wider variety of crops. These positive effects can be negated
if the chosen techniques are not suited to soil and slope conditions, or if the techniques
are not properly implemented. Farmers are not generally familiar with these
techniques, and therefore the environmental outcome rests on the quantity and quality
of extension assistance provided to them. The mitigation plan would be similar to that
for the irrigation program and would involve improved training and awareness
programs.
(c) Home Garden Intensification: This activity is environmentally neutral with respect to
the natural environment, and potentially positive with respect to human nutrition



56
7.17   The Livestock Development Program covers:
(a) The Improvement and Operation of Animal Health Clinics: This activity has a
potentially positive environmental effect by reducing the incidence of disease in the
livestock population.
(b) Vaccination of Chickens Against Newcastle Disease: This activity is
environmentally positive in terms of limiting the incidence of this disease.
(c) Cattle Distribution: This activity poses some inherent risk to the environment that
can be mitigated by appropriate planning, training, execution and monitoring.
Potential impacts to be avoided are: 1) the spread of disease by moving sick animals
to areas free of disease; 2) damage to forest areas from free-roaming animals or
forest clearance to open grazing areas; and 3) run-off ofwaste from
holding areas. The mitigation plan involves a number of steps including; inspecting
cattle for disease prior to shipment; ensuring that recipient villages and groups have
adequate land and feed within existing village lands; and training recipient farmers to
control animals, to avoid plowing steep hillsides, and to use manure for fertilizer. This
component will be monitored frequently for compliance.
7.18  Improvement of Farm to Market Roads. This sub-component has the greatest
potential among all SAADP activities to cause adverse environmental impacts. Potential
impacts are: 1) improper construction or alignment can lead to soil erosion from the road
surface or cut and fill slopes; 2) improperly sized or constructed culverts or bridges can
impair surface water flow, especially in wet land areas; and 3) improper siting would
provide access and may cause disturbance to protected areas. These risks are however
reduced because the roads are generally short and would follow existing alignments.
However, risks stemming from improper construction are increased by the fact that
construction will be carried out by villagers rather than experienced contractors. Site
screening, construction supervision, and monitoring are required as mitigation measures.
A project manual similar to the one being used for the Village Infrastructure Project will
be used to guide villages through the road design and construction process, and to insure
that proper construction standards are used. In addition a screening process would be put
in place whereby the individual roads proposed for funding each year are evaluated to
ensure that their siting would not cause significant adverse impacts.
7.19  Institutional Measures. In order to ensure that the appropriate monitoring
procedures are put in place and that environmental concerns become part of routine
decision-making, environmental management activities would be integrated into the
institutional structure of the project. Environmental screening will especially be imbedded
into the annual proposal review and budgeting process. An Environmental Specialist will
be recruited for 3 months during the first year of the project to formulate Environmental
Management and Monitoring Plan templates, develop environmental training modules,
make recommendations for detailed institutional arrangements, and assist the project



57
managers to operationalize environment related activities. As part of its annual program
review process, the Provincial Coordination Committee (PCC) would ensure the screening
of all project proposals, making sure that the districts are formulating and implementing
Environmental Management and Monitoring Plans as necessary in accordance with the
templates and regulations. The PCC can ask appropriately qualified institutions, such as
the Environmental Studies Center of the provincial university, to help it in the screening
process. The PCC would be responsible for implementing environmental training, and
monitoring the implementation of any management plans that may be necessary. Project
Management Units at district level would be responsible for ensuring that: 1) the
recommendations made by the screeners are incorporated into the annual proposal review
process; 2) the technical agencies have properly formulated and are following any
necessary environmental management and monitoring plans; and 3) the extension agents/
road engineers advising farmers/communities are trained to advise their clients how to plan
and implement project activities in an environmentally sound manner.
Impact on the Social and Cultural Environment.
7.20  The preparation process has included a social assessment study to establish an
initial socio-economic and cultural picture of the project areas and beneficiaries; assess the
impact of project activities on the local population and other stakeholders; examine and
find solutions for the local perceived constraints to agricultural development; and design
programs to meet aspirations of the local population. The assessment study recognizes
that though the two provinces are inhabited by diverse ethnic and linguistic groups no
serious social or cultural tensions have been reported in the target areas. Program design
and implementation would however need to be flexible and be adjusted to local conditions.
The principal conclusion of the assessment was that for the project to be sustainable
communities would have to participate fully in the planning and management process.
Project design therefore provides for this direct involvement of the local communities in
planning and implementation of project activities. The assessment study also reviewed the
possible impact of the different agricultural activities on the socio-economic situation of
the target areas. Though farmers in the project areas still adopt the cultural framework
associated with subsistence agriculture, it is not anticipated that any induced socio-
economic change would result in any deterioration of current social or cultural values.
Adoption of new technology by the project participants would be on a voluntary basis.
7.21  In order to maintain the high degree of community involvement required, the
project will finance community facilitators, to be recruited through CDOs. The latter are
expected to play a key role in identifying and implementation of operations under the
community based initiatives program, in farmer organization and training, and helping
farmer groups manage their revolving credit. For this purpose it has been agreed that a
draft standard-type agreement would be drawn up between the provincial authorities
(Bappeda TK I) in both provinces and reputable CDO(s) for the supply and management
of community development facilitators. The draft standard-type agreement would be
discussed during negotiations and the agreement signed before disbursements are made
on the community based initiatives program.



58
7.22  The project involves no resettlement and does not involve land acquisition for any
of the activities proposed either for field demonstration or the infrastructure components.
Demonstrations will be on farmers' fields to be managed by farmers themselves and road
construction would consist of upgrading of existing roads and tracks with no need for land
acquisition. Though the project would support local people indigenous to the two
provinces (as opposed to migrants) it does not involve any "indigenous" or "tribal"
peoples. None of the target areas appear to include locations of major monuments of
archaeological significance. Cultural habits and customs reflect a pattern of life that
centers on small scale farming activities. The project interventions would not change this
basic lifestyle, but aim to create a more sustainable pattern of land use and provide
technological options for improvement of agriculture and other economic activities.
Impact on Women and Youth
7.23  Reviewing rural conditions in Sulawesi confirms the central role played by women
in agricultural activities. Women and youth are involved in most aspects of the agricultural
production cycle from weeding and planting to harvesting and marketing in addition to
household tasks. Their responsibilities appear to be a lot more in foodcrop production and
house gardening than in estate crop production even though in the latter area they do most
of the harvesting, processing and marketing. They are normally responsible for small
livestock (chickens and ducks), less involved with goats and other small ruminants and not
usually involved in the husbandry of larger animals (cattle and water buffaloes). The
family concept appears to be very strong however and hence, women are reported to be
responsible for family finances.
7.24  The approach to be adopted regarding the different roles of men, women and
youth in the household, as well as in the production systems, is to make them equal
beneficiaries of all activities. Since the focus of project support would be on the
household, project interventions would need to address the specific needs of different
household members. Gender disaggregated data would have to be collected to obtain a
better picture of the role of various groups in the community and the household. The
project includes support for activities that are traditionally the responsibility of women,
such as home gardening, poultry vaccination, produce processing, marketing, small
enterprise development, etc. - the latter activities through the local initiatives component.
Other activities that can positively impact on women and youth also include provision of
credit, seeds and other inputs. Providing opportunities for increasing women and youth's
involvement in agricultural development, consistent with their active role in the sector, will
enhance their contribution to development. Gender sensitivity training for extension
workers, project staff, and community leaders will enhance their skills to tap this important
human resource and improve the participation of women and youth groups in the project.
Participation of women in the various project activities would be monitored in the M&E
program.



59
Project Risks
7.25   Three risks have been identified and taken into consideration in project formulation
and design:
(a)   Capacity of the Local Government Agencies. Sub-standard capabilities and
lack of experience and resources of these agencies could lead to delays in
achieving the implementation targets. The experience of NTASP, as indicated
in its ICR, has however shown that it is possible to work with inexperienced
local and provincial level institutions, strengthen them in their respective roles
in planning, implementing, and coordinating decentralized and multi-sectoral
interventions while at the same time providing useful services to farmers to
improve their productivity. The ICR of NTASP has also confirmed that the
focus on implementation through the existing administration structure at the
provincial, kabupaten and kecamatan levels, and a systematic effort for using
project area CDOs, are important and relevant approaches to ensure adequate
project impact. To minimize the risk, the project includes several training
activities for agency staff and community leaders at project start and
employment of technical assistance to assist in project implementation.
Operating means are also provided to ensure that staff are able to make
frequent field visits to plan and implement activities with farmers. The volume,
scale and complexity of project interventions would be planned from year to
year on the basis of a realistic assessment of institutional capacities, both in the
local Dinas and the participating CDOs.
(b)    Organizational structure and implementation arrangements. Institutional
and organizational complexity of the proposed project is another risk and may,
together with weaknesses of local technical agencies (referred to above), cause
implementation delays. This risk is reduced by defining clearly the
responsibilities of the various agencies involved in the project implementation
at all levels and using the existing structures and coordination arrangements
which would help project execution. Technical assistance would also help
improve the project management during the early stages of implementation.
(c)    Delays and possible abuse of the bottom-up process. The third risk involves
possible delays and even abuse of the bottom-up planning process that is to be
used to implement the agriculture activities. Local govenmment officials have
been used to implementing central sectoral operations for which instructions
are issued from the center. The proposed approach is therefore novel and may
take some time to implement. This risk is reduced by including CDOs, with
experience in community participation, in the farmer organization and planning
program. Also the first project year will be used for training of staff and
community leaders and to explain the project concept to them and to start field
level activities only in the second year.

INDONESIA
SULAWESI AGRICULTURAL AREA
DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Implementation of Agricultural Area Development
A. Village Irrigation
Background
1.    There are a large number of village irrigation schemes in the project provinces
which have been established by the farmers without government assistance. Yields of
paddy in these schemes average about 3.0 tons/ha due to the use of low quality seeds, low
levels of fertilizer, and poor management of water. The Government launched in January
1995 an ambitious three-year national program to upgrade village irrigation schemes covering
some 900,000 ha, or about 90 percent of all schemes believed to exist in the country. The
program is known as Improvement of Village Irrigation Schemes (PID), and sometimes also
referred to as Village Irrigation Crash Program. The objective of the program is to quickly
improve diversion structures and canals in the majority of the country's village irrigation
systems (including those located in the two project provinces) and thereby increase Indonesia's
paddy production by about one million ton. The works are designed and executed by the
Public Works Department, either under contract or force account. It is anticipated that this
program will help upgrade the irrigation infrastructure in most of the schemes currently
exploited by farmers. Additional assistance is however required to successfully transfer
improved production technology to farmers to achieve maximum economic yields.
Proposed Program
2.    The objective of the project intervention is to transfer appropriate production
technology for intensification of rice and soybeans/mungbeans cultivation through
demonstrations of technology packages in village irrigation schemes in the target areas.
Demonstrations (3.0 ha. per village irrigation scheme for two years) would be carried out
on farmers' fields with the active participation of farmer groups. Project assistance would
be provided in the form of extension advice, training and provision of seeds, inputs and
agricultural tools.
3.    The Dinas Food Crops at the kabupaten level, with technical backing from the two
AIAT centers in Kendari and Palu and the provincial agricultural services and in close
consultation with the farmers, would be responsible for implementation of the demonstration
programs. Over the six project years, a series of on-farm irrigated crop demonstrations,
totaling about 3 ha per scheme would be established in about 60 village irrigation schemes
averaging 50 ha each in Southeast Sulawesi and in some 142 schemes in Central Sulawesi.
Typically the 3 ha demonstration would be established in adjacent 0.5 ha farm plots involving



63                                 ANNEX 1
six farmers in each scheme and will be supported for two years. Dinas staff, working together
with extension agents and farmer groups, would identify farmers willing to volunteer their
plots and services for the demonstrations. They would then assist the farmer groups with the
preparation of detailed plans for implementation of the demonstrations and train the farner
leaders in the proposed new technologies. The Dinas would arrange for the provision to the
farmers of seeds, fertilizer, tools and equipment for two cropping cycles of paddy and a
palawija crop (or a second paddy crop where water supply is adequate). Dinas and CDO
staff also would encourage the participating farmers to form groups and to set up revolving
funds for the purchase of farm inputs at the end of project financing (after Year 2).
4.      AdoDtionlReplication in Village krri2ation. Credit will be made available under the
viUage revolving credit scheme (Section I below) to farmers to purchase recommended and
tested inputs. To establish the annual program for adoption, farmers would make proposals
annually through the village level planning process with support from their PPL and the
community development facilitators. The village level proposals, will be consolidated at
the kecamatan level for transmission to the Kabupaten level where the Dinas will review
and consolidate all proposals for inclusion in the annual work program. For purposes of
calculating the impact of the project, it is assumed that in addition to the areas under
demonstration (180 ha in SE and 420 ha in Central), about 80 percent of the PID improved
schemes (7,550 ha out of 10,500 ha) involving about 20,000 beneficiaries will avail themselves
of the project assistance through the credit program to improve their cultivation practices.

the PID program, farmers can increase their paddy yields from about 3 t/ha to about 4.5 t/ha
and soybean yields from 600 kg to 800 kg/ha. With the project intervention of improved
agricultural practices, and improved seeds, paddy yields are estimated to increase to 5.5 tons/ha
and soybeans to about 1,200 kg/ha. Estimates of incremental production are in Table A. 1 of
this Annex. A summary farm budget for a typical 0.5 ha plot of irrigated land is given in Table

B. Food Crops Seed Multiplication
Background
7.    The provision of high quality seeds is an important part of the development program
for food crops production. The Government of Indonesia, since the introduction of the BIMAS
(mass guidance) in the early sixties, has considered seed production to be one of the five major
aspects in foodcrops production improvement. Since its first launching in the mid- Pelita I
period (1971), the seed multiplication program has been supported by various Government
policies on seed certification. However, the implementation of such policies has faced major
constraints at provincial and kabupaten level, so that the availability of extension seeds at the
farming level is not sufficient, in particular in the more remote areas in Indonesia, away from
Java and Bali.
8.    Therefore, in supporting the development of Sulawesi Tenggara and Sulawesi Tengah,
especially in the foodcrops production program with the introduction of improved technologies
and locally adapted varieties, the production of sufficient high quality seeds for farmers use, is
very critical. The Kabupaten Seed Multiplication Centers for Food Crops (BBP) are a critical
link between the Main Seed Production Center at provincial level (BBU) and the farmer seed
growers. One of the setbacks of the Government's recent past foundation seed delivery
policies, was that most varieties were selected under Java conditions only, but approved for
wider distribution. Fortunately, AARD and MOA now realize that different agro-ecological
zones with different management requirements and pests and diseases, need different
characteristics in the varieties. Varieties acceptable to Java, may not be good enough for other
islands.
9.    At present both the production of stock seed from the BBPs and extension seed from
the Farmer Seed Growers provide less than half of the total seed requirements of farmers who
now rely mainly on low yielding local and degenerated HYV varieties. Often the same seed is
used in different seasons, while different varieties are required. Seed purity (durability) and
viability are often below standard, resulting in poor germination and diminished yields. The
Government's attention in seed supplies has been mainly on rice in the past. However for



65                           ANNEX 1
Sulawesi Tenggara and Tengah, other food crops such as maize, soybeans, mungbeans and
peanuts are equally important in the dryland farming system.
10.   Major constraints. The main constraints in the Indonesian seed industry, and on the
outer islands in particular, include:
* insufficient number of seed stations (private or public) in the country to produce and
multiply breeders seed;
* insufficient capacity and production of locally adapted, high quality provincial foundation
(BBI) and kabupaten stock (BBP) seeds to supply farmer seed growers;
* insufficient number of well trained extension seed producers/farmer seed growers;
* inadequate and poor organization of the Seeds Control and Certification Stations (SCCS);
*  insufficient recognition by the farmer users of the importance of using locally adapted, high
quality seeds and varieties in raising yields.
11.    The overall constraint, however, seems to be the strong govemment control in the
national seed production and delivery system, crowding out the private sector, and an
unattractive remuneration for breeders rights and the private seed sector.
Proposed Project intervention
12.   Since there is no viable private seed sector yet, the project will facilitate the production
of stock seeds at selected BBPs in the 8 project kabupatens, in order to supply sufficient high
quality seeds to farmer seed growers. These growers in tum will produce the much needed
improved extension (certified) seeds for the farmer users.
13.   Kabupaten Seed Multiplication Centers (BBPs) for Food Crops. The Project
would assist to improve the performance and functioning of one operational center per
kabupaten and provide funding for the rehabilitation of the center, provision of agricultural and
laboratory equipment to upgrade the existing operations and training of the center's staff
Centers which are mainly designed for rice multiplication and have no sufficient upland
themselves, would cooperate with the nearest BPP to use their land for the multiplication of
non-rice seeds, such as soybeans, maize, mungbeans and peanuts, depending on the farmers'
needs in the area. Each selected seed center would prepare an annual management and
production plan, to be approved by and contracted with the Provincial Coordinating
Committee and to be submitted to the Bank as part of the annual work program and budget
before financing is provided. The plan should provide information on the (expected) number of
cooperating farmer seed growers in its domain and should also include:
* operational program to produce the required seed based on available area;
*  cost of production of the seed and expected quantity to be produced;
*  proposals on how the stock seed will be marketed and expected sales revenue;
*  proposals on origin of foundation seed;
Estimated costs to the Project: some RP 50 million/kabupaten in the first two years after which
the centers should be self-financing.



66                           ANNEX 1
Farmer Seed Growers Groups
14.   Selected volunteer farmers in the project target areas, would be trained by Dinas Food
Crops and Horticulture (DGFCH) to become certified seed growers for the supply of sufficient
extension seeds to other farmers. The seed-growers would be selected in small groups of 4
members, covering some 2 ha together. Depending on the farmers' ability, the quality and land
use patterns of their land, and the importance of the crop in the surrounding production area,
these groups would decide what major crop to multiply. In predominantly rice growing areas,
most groups would opt for rice or soybean, while in the dryland areas, maize, mungbeans and
peanut seeds would be more in demand. It is assumed that for rice and soybeans, there would
be one 2 ha seed growers' group per three irrigation villages, while for the other crops, there
would be one group per five participating villages. All these groups would initially be financed
under the Project. However, any farmer interested in seed production, should be able to
receive the training under the project to upgrade his/her own seed.
15.   Moreover it is strongly recommended to organize the seed growers in Seed-Growers
Associations with direct links, through contracts, to the BBP seed multiplication center for the
regular supply of high quality stock seeds and with the SCCS, for their timely field inspections
and certification. The project's cost estimates per ha of extension seed production for 2 years
for rice, soybeans, mungbeans and peanuts, varies from RP 1.1 to RP 1.3 million. Because of
the relative complexity of maize breeding, it is foreseen that initially most maize seeds have to
come from the BBU and the private sector.
C. Dryland Farming System Development
Background.
16.   The productivity of the prevailing smallholder systems of dryland agricultural
production in the target areas is low as are farm incomes. In addition, the practice of shifting
cultivation in some areas is leading to degradation of land resources. One of the most
important results that came from the Farming Systems Research program of the Nusa
Tenggara Agricultural Services Project (NTASP) was the necessity to stress the use of
conservation farming techniques in the fragile soils of eastern Indonesia and to link these
techniques to other farming activities such as livestock production. Because population
densities of Southeast and Central Sulawesi are still low, there has been a tendency to assume
that there is an abundance of unused land and that therefore, the need for conservation
practices to preserve soil fertility is not as important as in overcrowded Java. However given
the fragile and less productive soils of the East, it is in general even more important there to
practice conservation farming to preserve the shallow topsoils. Technology packages for
dryland farming systems development have been tested in many parts of Indonesia including in
some parts of Sulawesi and can be promoted in the target areas. Some commnon forms of
dryland farming techniques are the following: Alley Cropping (Tanaman Lorong); Contour
Ridge Terracing (Teras Guludan); Cover Crops; Living Fences (Pagar Hidup); and Bench



67                          ANNEX 1
Terraces (Teras Bangku). In addition to these techniques promoting soil conservation, they
have the added advantage of providing additional fodder for animal feed and producing
fuelwood.
Project Intervention
17.   The objective of the intervention would be to introduce improved dryland farming
systems technology packages to increase farmer incomes and reduce negative environmental
effects from traditional cultivation systems. Diffusion of farming systems development
technology packages to area farmers would be carried out through provision of assistance to
farmers interested in adopting the technology. Project assistance would be provided for
demonstration, seedling/seed production, and replication. The main technique to be
promoted would be aDley cropping of lamtoro/gamal-vetiver/king grass, inter-cropping of food
crops with improved seeds and minimum level of inputs, and establishment of low density
plantings of cashew or other appropriate perennial crops. Nurseries to produce seedlings
would be implemented by the farmer groups with inputs being provided by the project. The
farmers would provide all labor without compensation.
18.   Demonstrations. The suitability of the technology packages would be demonstrated
in field demonstrations which would be established in each of the target areas. It is proposed to
have approximately a one 3 ha on-farm demonstration for each 3 project villages for a total of
380 demonstrations covering 1,140 ha. Each demonstration would involve a minimum of 4
farmers with an average farm size of 0.75 ha each. Support in the form of inputs will be
provided for two years. The executing agency would depend on the final design of the
technology package for each target area. If foodcrops are dominant in the farming systems
package, then the Dinas TK II Food Crops would be the local executing agency. If estate
crops are locally dominant, then Dinas TK II Estate Crops would be the executing agency. The
design of each demonstration would be done with support from the AIAT. Participating
farmers would receive all inputs free but be expected to provide the necessary labor as their
contribution. For each demonstration as well as replication, the project intervention would
consist of the following activities:
*  review of existing dryland areas and identification of priority areas for dryland farming
systems development activities;
*  preparation of location specific farming systems technology packages appropriate for each
area with technical input from staff from the Assessment Institute for Agricultural
Technology and discussions with area farmers;
*  training of farmer group leaders on dryland conservation farming systerns techniques
*  preparation of detailed plans and proposals for assistance for dryland farming system
development with farmer participation to be submitted through the LKMD and the bottom-
up planning system; and
*  provision of extension advice and a package of farm tools and inputs for up to 0.75 ha./
farmer during two years to farmers to assist them to establish areas on their own land using
the farming systems development technology



68                                 ANNEX 1
19.    Replication. In order to ensure that farmers who want to adopt and replicate
project activities and project introduced technology have the means to do so, the project
includes a provision for a village level revolving credit that would make funds available to
farmer groups for input purchase (Section I). The project would finance inputs for farmer
groups based on a clearly determined program of replication that they would establish with
support from the extension agents and community facilitators. Farmer group selection in
each village will be based on willingness of the group to adopt the recommended technical
package but where several groups are involved group selection will be done by a joint
commnittee including at least the Dinas for foodcrops, treecrops and in some cases the
Livestock and/or Fisheries Dinas, as well as a BRI representative, and village hamlet
heads (average of about 4 per village). It is expected that the PPLs and the CDFs will
help selected farmer groups prepare proposals for replication of project-introduced
technology
20.    The package to be financed for replication would include seedlings for the tree crop
considered suitable for the area, improved food crop seeds, tools and a minimum supply of
fertilizers and agro-chemicals for the tree crop seedlings and food crops. High yielding
varieties of food crop seeds would be procured from contract growers to be supported under
the project. Seedlings for the tree crops would be raised in private village nurseries that the
project would assist village communities to set up. Table 2 below gives the progression of

Expected Results
21.   The selected drylandfarm model includes inter-cropped treecrops, including fruit-
trees, annual foodcrops (but no padi gogo) and fodder crops, including king grass and
leguminous trees; basic principles of soil conservation and maintenance of soil fertility will be
applied. Farmers are expected to shift from ladang and upland rice to permanent dryland
farming and maize. Cashew is the tree crop of choice since cashew is: (a) expected to be
suitable to many of the areas, (b) popular among the farmers, and (c) representative for the
costs and benefits of other tree crops. High value fruit and nut trees (kemiri, mango, citrus) are
also expected to be favored and in a few cases cocoa may be a viable tree crop alternative.
Three different types of models were analyzed under the following assumptions: Model I - 40
percent of project farmers would adopt this approach made up mainly of foodcrops plus fodder
and leguminous trees. There is no fertilizer use and yields are low as a consequence. Model 2
- also 40 percent of project farmers, includes cashew as the treecrop of choice, and moderate
use of fertilizer. Model 3 - 20 percent of project farmers, includes fruit trees as the 'tree crop"
and also fair use of fertilizer. Table A. 1 shows the component's estimated incremental
production under the above assumptions.
D. Village Treecrop Nurseries.
22.   Within the Ministry of Agriculture, the DGEC is responsible for the provision of
seedlings of commercial treecrops, such as rubber, oil palm, cashew, coconut, cacao, coffee
and tea, both to estates and smallholders. The DGFHC is responsible for the provision of all
other non-forest trees, including fruits, nuts and ornamental trees, mainly for homegarden use.
Improved treecrop varieties and clones are generated by specialized treecrops research centers
under AARD and the private sector. Except for coconuts (Manado) and oil palm (Medan),
most of the commercial treecrops research centers are located in Java. The center for fruit-trees
research is in Solok, Sumatra. As a consequence, the supply of improved treecrop seedlings to
Sulawesi Tenggara and Tengah, is very poor. Both Dinases (Estate and Foodcrops) have
scattered nurseries, mainly near existing BPPs, but cannot supply seedlings in sufficient
quantities to support the project in extending the inter-cropped dryland farming systems models
to reduce swidden agriculture, make dryland farming more permanent and improve/expand
conservation practices. Moreover, the quality of the seedlings is seldom uniform and need to
be improved (e.g. by using grafted rootstocks).
23.   Private nurseries of fruit and omamental trees can be found adjacent to the bigger cities
of Kendari and Palu. However in the more remote areas, seedlings are scarce. Therefore it is
proposed that under the Project, both Dinas-Dinas K II for estate and horticultural treecrops,
assist participating village communities to establish their own, privately run, village nursery.
These nurseries would then provide the needed seedlings at a reasonable price to farmers who
are willing to adopt the integrated crop production system or alley cropping and plant trees
along the field contours. The same nurseries would provide fruit trees and other seedlings to
the home gardens in the village. Therefore both Dinas-Dinas will have to play an important
role in the establishment of these multi-purpose nurseries and teach farmer owners the



70                                 ANNEX 1
operational and financial management and maintenance of their new enterprises. Initially all
inputs and seeds for the establishment and operation in the first two years, would be provided
under the project after which period the nursery has to be self financing through its sales of
seedlings. The total costs of the establishment of such a village nursery of 0.2 ha is estimated at
RP 2.5 million over two years. Under the Project, it is foreseen that one nursery may supply
seedlings for two neighboring village communities.
E. Treecrop Rehabilitation.
24.    Average yields of smaliholder cocoa and cashew in the target areas are low since
smallholder cultural practices (pruning, weeding and fertilization) for these crops are generally
poor. The area under smallholder cacao is about 25,000 ha in Central Sulawesi and 71,500 ha
in Southeast Sulawesi, while the area under cashew is 123,500 ha in Southeast and less than
8,000 ha in Central. The objective of this intervention would be to demonstrate to smallholders
in the target areas improved management techniques to rehabilitate existing plantings of cacao
or cashew. Project assistance would be provided for demonstration through inputs, planting
material and small tools and for replication through the village level credit system.
Demonstrations would be implemented on farmer fields and farmers would supply all labor.

Technical Intervention and Expected Results
26.    For cacao it is recommended to apply proper farm management (including the use of
fertilizer and agro-chemicals) and pruning techniques to improve tree shapes, fruit bearing,
crop sanitation and ventilation and improve the quality of the shade trees by planting lamtoro
and gamal trees. There is not expected to be any inter-planting with foodcrops and
recommended tree densities for mature cacao will be 400 - 500 trees/ha. Yields from the
rehabilitation are projected to go up from 300 kg/ha (without project situation) to about 800
kg/ha five years after rehabilitation.



72                          ANNEX 1
27.   For cashew it is recommended to reduce the number of mature trees to some 33
trees/ha and plant foodcrops in between, such as corn and mungbeans. Alternatively high
quality cashew seedlings (40) can be planted to replace the ones which have been cut out,
which in turn will replace the oldest trees after some 4 to 6 years. In the meantime the AIAT in
Kendari will, with the assistance of Bogor and the Directorate General of Estate Crops (they
have a small collection on East Java), establish a superior 5 ha cashew garden for clonal
production (vegetative multiplication through grafting and budding). Most of the mother trees
material will be collected on the islands of Buton and Muna. Table A. I includes the estimated
increnental production from the rehabilitation program.
F. House Plot Development
Background
28.   Almost all rural households have house plots of 0.1 to 0.2 ha. However, the
productivity of these house plots tends to be low. There is significant potential to intensify
cultivation on these plots and introduce production of more fruits, vegetables, fuel wood, and
forage crops on these home gardens.
Proposed Developments
29.   Home garden cultivation would be improved under the project in order to improve
family nutrition and raise incomes. Intensification of cultivation would be carried out through
training of community leaders, demonstrations and replication through the revolving credit
system. Project assistance would be provided in the form of extension advice, seedlings, seeds,
inputs, and small tools. There will be a total of 360 ha of demonstrations (equivalent to about
1 demo of 0.2 ha each per villages in the target area).
30.   The Dinas Food Crops TK. II would implement this activity. The extension workers
would cooperate closely with the PKK in each location. The following activities would be
undertaken: (a) design of location specific technology packages in consultation with the
villagers, especially the women; (b) training of community leaders (in particular PKK) in
upgrading of home plots and sanitation; (c) provision of inputs for demonstrations; and (d)
assistance for villagers who also wish to upgrade their house plots.
Expected Results
31.   It is expected that 1800 households would participate in the demonstrations while an
additional 7,000 households would replicate the program. Estimates of incremental production
are in Table A. I.



73                           ANNEX 1
G. Livestock Development
Background
32.   Total livestock population in the two project areas consists of 1.2 million cattle, 56,000
buffalo, 340,000 small ruminants, approximately a quarter million pigs, and nearly 10 million
native poultry. Other important species include waterfowl and pigeons. Livestock contributes
approximately $ 5.5 million (4.4%) to regional GDP. The southern peninsula (South Sulawesi)
is a major cattle growing area where approximately 20% of the households depend on cattle
production as a major source of income, either derived from breeding, fattening, draught labor
and, indirectly, manure. Many of the agricultural areas in Central and Southeast Sulawesi,
however, have a low livestock population, leaving the rural population, without the buffer of
livestock ownership, more vulnerable to the risk of rainfed agriculture.
Lessons from past Distribution Programs.
33.   Livestock distribution started with the transmigration programs in the late seventies.
The major objective were to provide farmers with production and security means, by correcting
the two major market failures in transmigration areas e.g. lack of livestock markets, and the
lack of credit. Livestock were provided through an in kind credit systems whereby farmers
receive livestock and had to return two of the offspring within a given time period. Overall the
program has been successful in the distribution. The major target animal has been cattle, but
projects have also distributed goats as well as poultry. Overall the programs have been
appreciated by farmers. The perceived benefits were: improved welfare and increased house
hold security due to increased savings; increased productivity, production intensity and
security from use of manure and draught power; regular income from calf fattening (young
bulls 6-12 months).
34.   A recent review of the livestock distribution programs showed a number of problems
including: (i) bureaucratic delays in repayment in kind (e.g. farmers waiting till they can deliver
calf, with associated risks), (ii) inadequate animal health and services especially in remote areas,
(iii) lack of adequate feed in the dry season (mainly in drier areas), and (iv) the lack of
knowledge about animal husbandry of recipient farmers (and the lack of appropriate extension
help). In some areas farmers also complained about the lack of breeding service and/or access
to bulls. In addition most distribution systems have worked very much top down and have
rarely involved farmers in the planning and processing.
35.   There exist some experience with livestock distribution in the region, most recently in
an IFAD financed project that distributed livestock in a selected number of kabupaten both in
Central and Southeast Sulawesi. Among the recognized problems especially in the area in
previous distribution schemes was the lack of proper preparation of farmers, the lack of
sufficient training and technical assistance/supervision, lack of animal health services and other
institutional problems. The proposed project will draw on this experience but will be different
especially with respect to a greater decentralization of decision making and management.



74                           ANNEX 1
Project Intervention
36.   Management of the Distribution System. The project would provide support for
distribution of breeding cattle, promotion of intensive management systems, and establishment
of community-based revolving systems for redistribution of cattle in support of future farming
systems development activities. In each of the eight Kabupaten the Dinas TK II Livestock
would be responsible for implementing the distribution activities. In each kecamatan, a
working group for livestock distribution would be formed by the local Dinas TK II Livestock
to be composed oftheMantf' Ternak, PPLs for livestock, staff of the Animal Health Clinics,
village chiefs, and representatives of farmer groups, the kecamatan Community Development
Facilitator (CDF), and staff of Dinas TK II Livestock as necessary to be responsible for the
general supervision of the livestock distribution and credit system. Within the group will be a
livestock credit committee (made up of the community leaders) that will be specifically
responsible for managing the credit program (see Section I below). Under the supervision of
the Dinas TK  /PII[PRO, this group would be responsible for: selecting the desa's and farmer
groups to receive cattle and for cattle procurement. They will also be responsible for training
of the farrner groups; distribution of cattle to farmer groups; monitoring of the health of the
cattle distributed; and ensuring the repayment of the revolving credit. The PPL and kecamatan
level staff of the Dinas will be responsible for the provision of livestock/forage extension
services and veterinary services; maintenance of records on the performance (calving rates and
death losses) and supervision of the redistribution of cattle.
37.   The livestock distribution group will identify the areas where the livestock distribution
will take place and explain the criteria for receiving the livestock to potential beneficiaries.
Priority would be given to farmers meeting the following main criteria:
*  having developed or already using sustainable farm practices
*  not having owned livestock before
*  having expressed interest, and subsequently followed all the training
*  agreeing to develop the needed fencing and stables
*  having enough fodder crops planted to feed two animals year around, depending on
the area this may include 250 m of forage trees in hedge rows, or .25 ha of King
grass, or equivalent; and
*  having a farm income below the average for the kecamatan
38.   Before the program starts, the following main training will take place:
pre-planning Training. The Livestock Dinas, PPL's and others involved are to be
trained in: (i) interaction with farmers, (ii) farm business planning; (iii) training methods
e.g. tools, methods; (iv) technical issues relating to integrated farmning systems; (v)
technical issues relating to livestock.



75                           ANNEX 1
pre-reception training of farmers that will consist of a three month, one meeting/week
training of the group in sustainable fodder crop cultivation, animal handling, animal
feeding, stable/fence construction, basic animal health etc.;
39.   Criteria in livestock selection should include size, age, weight and health condition.
Selection would be made by a group consisting of at least a representative of the Dinas TKII
livestock (PIMPRO), a representative of the recipient farmers groups, an experienced farner
(but not an official) from the same or adjacent area who has participated in earlier cattle
schemes, PPL with experience in cattle scheme and, where necessary a livestock expert of the
kabupaten or provincial Dinas. The suggested minimum requirements for cattle are:
Criteria            Bali breed           Local breed
Age                  1.5 years           1.5 years
Height male          105 cm              110 cm
female        102 cm              105 cm
Weight               150 Kg              180 Kg
Health              Certified            Certified
Status              Vaccinated           Vaccinated
40.   For the procurement of the cattle, it is expected that some farmer groups would
want to shop locally for their breeding animals while others may want the assistance of the
project to procure the animals. It is proposed therefore to allow half of the beneficiaries
to acquire their cattle through local shopping and procure the other half (US$1.5 million)
by NCB procedures and using a model bid document acceptable to the Bank. In the latter
case, the animals would be procured by the Dinas Livestock TK. II to the extent possible from
the local area and as close as possible to the distribution areas in order to: (i) limit the risk of
introducing new diseases etc.; (ii) reduce the stress of shipping on animals, and improve the
overall condition and survival rate (after transport); and (iii) procure animals that are common
to the area, and to many of the farmers, and extension staff.
41.    Some 4,400 cattle (females) and 440 bulls would be procured for distribution in
Central Sulawesi, and about 4,000 cattle and 400 bulls in Southeast. In addition, a total of
2,728 animals (2,480 cattle and 248 bulls) are expected to be redistributed in the two provinces
from calf repayments through the revolving credit system. Cattle distribution will be to groups
of 10 farmers each at the ratio of one bull for every ten heifers.
42.   The distribution of animals would start during the second project year, which would
allow sufficient time for selection and training of the beneficiaries and the planting of fodder
trees and grass. The project would establish a revolving system to redistribute cattle to
disadvantaged area farmers. Each recipient would be allowed to keep the first off-spring but
would have to return the second and third off-springs. Priority for receiving redistributed
animals would be given to other low income farmers among the participating farmer groups.
Future redistribution would be used to support future farming systems development efforts in
the targeted kecamatans.
