Expected Results
43.   Cattle distribution would provide a source of direct income to the participants through
the sale of offspring, as well as through their use for draft and transport purposes and the
production of manure. Fodder production would be an integral part of the conservation
farming system promoted under the project. The herd projections and the annual animal sales
quantities and values, without and with the project, are in Tables 4 and 5 ?, respectively
H. Poultry vaccination.
44.   Background. The most widely owned livestock in the target areas are chickens which
are raised in the home gardens of almost every rural household. Newcastle disease is a
common disease of chickens in the target areas and causes death losses of up to 80 % of
smallholder chickens annually. Control of this disease is therefore considered to be a high
priority by rural households, but access to vaccines and local knowledge on the use of vaccines
is limited. Though the government has made some effort to promote poultry vaccination
against Newcastle disease the effect has been limited by: (a) the need for refrigeration of the
vaccines; (b) the requirement of injections; and (c) the limited budgets for vaccines and
vaccination.
Project Intervention
45.   The project would provide support for training of area farners and especially women
in the techniques of vaccination of chickens against Newcastle disease. In each village
requesting assistance, vaccines would be provided (on a grant basis to the village but payable
by beneficiaries) during two years. The Dinas Livestock TK. II would coortinate this activity
and assist the women organization, the PKK (Family Welfare Organization) to implement the
vaccination program. In order to overcome limitations caused by the need for refrigeration, the
project is to introduce the new technology of a heat resistant vaccine ( the V4 strain) now
available in Indonesia. The project would fund initial introduction ofthe vaccine and training
of women groups in its use in the target.
Expected Results
46.   The vaccination program would be implemented in all the 900 villages, where an
estimated 650,000 chickens will be vaccinated during the two year campaign. Losses from
Newcastle disease would be minimized and a capacity would be established to continue with
the vaccination program after the project is closed.



77                           ANNEX 1
1. Revolving Credit Program
47.   The program is expected to make available to farmers funds that would enable them to
adopt the recommended technologies and agricultural programs as well as undertake
agricultural processing and marketing. While the basic objective of the program would be to
ensure that farrners are able to replicate the agricultural programs, it was agreed at negotiations
that up to 40 percent of the grant available to each village may be used for agricultural
marketing or processing. Thus funds will be made available to villages as a Grant for on-
lending to farmer groups and members of the village essentially for the following:
* agricultural activities (seeds, seedlings, agricultural inputs, agricultural tools, animal
traction, etc .....
livestock production (forage production, poultry, ....),
* agricultural processing and trading
The following procedures and operating principles will be used for operating the village
revolving credit.
48.   Bank and Government Financing. Under the replication program, beneficiaries
from participating villages would use the funds received under the program to acquire the
seasonal inputs necessary for replication, and in later years, to develop alternative sources
of income. Beneficiary villages will on-lend the funds to farmer groups and individual
farmers who would have to repay the credit to the village to constitute the village-level
revolving credit. Similarly the villages may on-lend up to 40 percent of the Grant to
members of the village for agricultural marketing or processing. Bank financing will cover
70 percent of the grant to the village, while Government funding will pay for the remaining
30 percent.
49.    Credit Committee. For each village interested in using the revolving credit program,
a village credit committee will be set up including the village head, and the dusun heads. Each
village will have to include at least one woman in the committee. In all cases, where none of
the dusun heads or village head is a woman the women groups of the village will nominate one
woman from the village to serve on the committee. For each given year, the credit committee
will have the primary responsibility for reviewing credit applications and approving requests for
financing. For the replication program, the review of applications would be done in
consultation with the Dinas for foodcrops, treecrops and in some cases the Livestock
and/or Fisheries Dinas. Subject to the general credit conditions described in paras 51 to
53 below, the committee will also decide on the credit terms (amount, duration, interest and
repayment period), and ensure the repayment and subsequent on-lending to other groups in the
village.
50.   Eligibility and Credit Amount. In principle all members in a village, interested in
adopting the technology packages or in agricultural marketing or processing, will be eligible to
apply for the credit. However the maximum grant each village can obtain per year for



78                          ANNEX 1
onlending to its members will be Rp. 10 million with the maximum over the life of the
project of Rp. 25 million.
51.   Application and Funding Procedures for Credit. With the help of the village
extension workers and the community development facilitators, farmer groups will prepare
projects for adopting the technological packages being promoted by the project or for
marketing or processing of agricultural produce as the case may be. Initial project proposals
(especially for replication) will be prepared annually, will be consolidated at the Kecamatan
level and transmitted to the Kabupaten for review by the Kabupaten coordinating comrnittee
and included in the annual work program for the approval of the provincial coordinating
committees. After the approval of the work program, the Kabupaten committee will inform
each kecamatan which in turn will inform each village credit committee of the proposals
retained for financing for the coming year.
52.   Towards the time when funding is needed, the concerned farmers will submit their
request for funding, that would include the activities and inputs they want financed, to the
village credit committee. After the approval of the committee the request would be transmitted
to the corresponding Dinas PIMPRO (foodcrops, estate crops or Livestock) depending on the
group's activity either directly or through the Kecamatan Dinas depending on whichever is
easier. The PIMPRO would then submit the request for funding to the project coordinator.
Funds may then be given to the village committee for on-lending to the farmer group. If a
village committee so desires, the project can also make direct payments to input suppliers.
Each village applying for a grant will have to show: (i) for purposes of replication, the names of
the farmers and/or farmer groups with the agricultural activities they want to undertake, the
areas involved, and the inputs they want to purchase; and (ii) for purposes of marketing and
processing, the village will present an indicative budget of the activity;
53.   Repayments. Repayment would be after the planting season and loan duration
would not exceed one year. Repayments would be made to the village credit committee
which will be required to open a special account at nearest financial institution, including a
commercial bank orpos giro. The minimum interest rate to be charged by the village
would be 15 percent per year in the initial years and a higher rate in the latter years.
However even in the earlier years the villages can charge higher interest rates if they so
wish. Funds repaid would remain in the village for on-lending to other members of the
village (operating in this way quite similar to the fund allocation under the Government's
IDT program).
54.   Peer pressure within the village setting (and other village groups waiting to benefit
from the repayment) is expected to lead to high rates of reimbursement as is the case
under the Hamlet Credit program of the Yogya Upland Agriculture Development Project.
In addition, as part of the process of ensuring full repayment, villages with farmer groups
or individuals having overdue loans may not be provided further grants. CDOs working in
the project will assist in setting up the credit program and in training the credit committee to
manage the credit.



79                             ANNEX 1
55.    Livestock Revolving Credit. Some similar arrangements as the above input
credit will be made to cover the livestock revolving credit scheme. Past experience seems
to suggest that schemes that rely on the government technical agencies and BRI to police
repayment and redistribution are not completely sustainable after projects are completed.
Farmer groups and communities will play a more important role in managing the livestock
credit than has been done in previous livestock revolving schemes (especially the IFAD
financed projects). Even though BRI may be used as the channeling bank by virtue of its
presence in more rural areas than any other Bank, it is proposed that the contract for the
livestock credit be signed between the individual farmer and Kecamatan livestock
committees 1/ to be set up (regrouping heads of villages). The livestock committees will
be responsible for ensuring recuperation of the livestock credit and redistribution to
farmers who have not yet received the cattle and who meet the criteria to receive it. In
such a scheme, the role of BRI becomes that of an ordinary commercial bank.
J. Farm Access Roads
Background
56.    Poor road access and lack of other social infrastructure are major constraints for the
development of much of the eastem islands, including the two Sulawesi provinces. Many
villages still are not connected by all-weather roads to the provincial and kabupaten road
network and the condition of these district and provincial roads are also often poor. Existing
roads are often damaged by rain causing transportation breakdowns between villages and
markets. In the face of these needs, both the central and local Govemments have been
allocating large proportions of their development budgets to the upgrading of public
infrastructure. Improvement of transportation alone represented over 50 percent of regional
development expenditures in both target provinces during the Repelita V period. Despite these
past expenditures on road infrastructure, a recent OECF-financed study, entitled "Rural Areas
Infrastructure Development" (March 1994), estimates the cost of upgrading of village roads
and bridges in the "Desa Tertinggal" (IDT) villages2 at about US$40 million in Central
Sulawesi and US$17 million in Southeast Sulawesi.
57.    As part of the overall IDT program, OECF agreed to allocate about US$200 million
equivalent for the upgrading over two years (1995/96 and 1996/97) of rural infrastructure in
over 3,000 villages in 21 provinces, including the two Sulawesi provinces.3 Villages are being
selected in clusters of five of which at least three should be [DT villages. An amount of
US$65,000 equivalent is made available to each selected village for the construction of roads
and bridges, water supply and sanitation systems, and jetties. Existing Government procedures
l One could consider a Kecamatan committee per every 8 to 10 villages with each committee having a
representative from one of the villages.
2 About 51 percent of the villages in the Central Sulawesi project areas, and 59 percent in Southeast, are listed as
IDT villages.
3 A similar project, the Village Infrastructure Project (VIP) being financed by the Bank is under implementation in
Java.



80                           ANNEX 1
will be followed for approval, financing and implementation of the program. A special
INPRES4 will be allocated to the kabupaten and the works will be executed by contractors.
Consultants/supervising engineers employed under the OECF financed project will assist the
Dinas Public Works TK. II with the preparation of proposals and the design and supervision of
the works.
Project Intervention.
58.   Like in the rest of the project provinces, the condition of access roads, tracks and trails
to agricultural production areas in the targeted kecamatans is very poor. Access by motor
vehicles and carts is constrained and frequently farmers must carry all inputs and output from
the fields due to poor condition of the access roads. The project therefore provides for
improving the physical access to the agricultural production areas where village irrigation,
dryland farming systems development and estate crop demonstration activities have been
implemented. Project assistance would be provided to support design and improvement of
existing tracks, trails and rural roads that provide access to agricultural production areas. It is
expected that a substantial part of the budget allocation will be used for the design and
construction of bridges and culverts on existing rural roads and tracks which are critically in
need of improvement. Farm access roads would be upgraded to compacted aggregate surface
roads for use by motorcycles, animal drawn carts and four wheel vehicles. The alignments
would be made on existing foot-paths, so there would be no need to acquire land.
59.   In order to get the maximum participation of the population in the road
construction program, the construction program will be implemented by the village
resilience group, LKMD as is currently being done under the village infrastructure projects
(VIP) being financed by the Bank and OECF. Design and technical support would be
provided by the TK II Public Works Dinas in each Kabupaten. The construction manual,
already prepared for the VIP project in Bahasa Indonesia, will be adapted during the first
project year (by March 31, 1997) for use in implementing the component. A consultant
rural engineer/contracts specialist will be recruited by the project to oversee the
construction program and help with procurement and contracting. The consultant will be
responsible for preparing the project specific manual based on the VIP model and on
discussions with project beneficiaries.
Expected Results
60.   A total of about 660 kilometers of farm access roads would be upgraded to improve
access to village irrigation schemes, dryland farm systems production areas and estate crops
areas. Upgrading of farm to market roads would improve access to agricultural production
areas so that farmers spend less time per trip to their fields and are able to increase the
frequency of trips. Costs of transport of inputs and farm output would be effectively reduced.
It is proposed that the maximum allocation for each participating kecamatan (25 each in
4A development program funded through a special authorization by Presidential Instruction.



81                          ANNEX 1
Southeast and Central) should not exceed Rp 450 million (US$200,000) though it could be
less.
K. Local Community Initiatives
Background
61.   The bottom-up planning system, as specified in Ministry of Home Affairs Regulation
Number 9 of 1982, provides for the preparation of proposals for development activities from
the village level with screening at the higher levels. The system has the potential to improve
planning of project activities in order to respond more directly to the needs of the local people.
The main constraints of this planning system are: (a) insufficient capacity at the village and
kecamatan level to prepare proposals which are in line with the felt needs of the rural groups,
and (b) insufficient discretionary funding at the kabupaten level to respond to these proposals.
Lessons from Experience
62.   The social assessment undertaken as part of project preparation reviewed the
experience of different types of development programs financed in the two provinces. The
general conclusion was that for projects to be sustainable, they require a high degree of
community involvement and appropriation. Lessons indicate however that communities have
seldom fully participated in the management of community-based development (CBD)
programs. Social preparation to secure community commitment to and participation is very
important and help has to be provided to most communities in developing initiatives due to
weak village organizational structures. Similarly government officials need to be trained in
community-based initiatives.
63.   An approach that supported the development of community initiatives was tried under
the NTASP financed by the Bank. The experience showed that significant benefits can be
achieved from this approach. The ICR for NTASP5 observed that the overall development
impact and sustainability of this component were quite good but would have been enhanced if
program development had applied more rigorous criteria to define priority rankings for
interventions.
Project Intervention
64.   The proposed project would support local community initiatives which are expected to
result in direct benefits to the communities. Local leaders would be trained in community-
based planning methods and support will be provided for farmer and community organization.
The project would provide discretionary funding to support implementation of activities which
are considered to be a high priority by the local community and are technically and
economically feasible and environmentally sound. Though no specific limit has been set, it is
expected that each kecamatan would receive about Rp 90 million during the project
5Implementation Completion Report for NTASP, June 1995.



82                           ANNEX 1
(equivalent to some Rp 22.5 million or US$10,000 annually for four years). Generally it is
expected that a range of activities would be proposed in line with local conditions and
community preferences. Priority would be given to proposals for activities to promote farming
systems development which might include, but would not be limited to: revolving credit
systems for, water buffaloes, goats and pigs; introduction of new agricultural technology; post-
harvest processing and storage for food and estate crops; activities to control wild pigs; home
industries; and community managed infrastructure development activities (mostly water supply
and roads).
65.   Proposals would be made annually through the village level planning process and with
support from community development facilitators to be recruited under the project. The village
level proposals, formulated by farmer groups and/or villages with assistance from the
community development facilitators and/or CDOs, will be consolidated at the Kecamatan level
and reviewed to prioritize those with the greatest socio-economic impact and those expected to
improve rural incomes. The purpose of the review will be to ensure that proposed activities
meet the necessary criteria and to rank the activities coming from the kecamatan by order
of priority. The proposals will be forwarded to the Kabupaten, where the project steering
committee would review the proposals to ensure that they conform to the agreed criteria for
the component.
66.   Guidelines for selection of activities for funding would be established so as not to
delete the intent of eliciting priorities as identified by the community. A guide in Bahasa
Indonesia would be prepared as part of the training program for Community Development
Facilitators. To be selected for support by the project, the proposed schemes should meet the
following criteria:
o technically feasible and economically justified productive activities;
*  activities have to be identified by the population to be of high priority;
-  the population has to demonstrate that the activity is in their manifest interest by their
willingness to contribute at least their labor, land and local materials where necessary to the
realization of the activity;
  the population has to demonstrate their willingness to provide for the operation and
maintenance of activities financed under the sub-component.
67.   In addition the following limits are expected to apply:
(a)    maximum amount per scheme: Rp 10,000,000;
(b)    maximum amount per beneficiary: Rp 250,000;
(c)    minimum number of beneficiaries per scheme will be 10 households except for
sparsely populated areas where the minimum shall be 3 households.



83                         ANNEX 1
68.   Preference would be given to proposals which:
(a)   benefit the poorest segment of the community
(b)   involve women and youth groups;
(b)   are assisted by local CDOs;
(c)   have the lowest risk of failure;
(d)   have the greatest socio-economic impact; and
(e)   have the highest proposed contribution by the community
69.   Expected Results. The selected activities would measurably increase the incomes
and levels of welfare among the low income groups in the target areas. It is expected that
a minimum of 25,000 rural households would benefit from this activity.

93                          ANNEX 2
INDONESIA
SULAWESI AGRICULTURAL AREA
DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Regional Assessment Institutes for Agricultural Technology (AIATs)
Background
1.    The main body for agricultural research in Indonesia is the Agency for Agricultural
Research and Development (AARD) which was established by Presidential Degree in 1974.
AARD is designated to coordinate, manage and conduct agricultural research and development
all over Indonesia. To adapt to the changing demands of the agricultural sector, AARD's role
has been evolving over time and is being assisted to meet this under the Bank assisted Second
Agricultural Research Management Project (ARMP II).
2.    The objective of ARMP II is to: (a) improve the efficiency and cost effectiveness of
research and development(R&D) through decentralization of services to the regions, and (b)
focus on specific agro-ecological zones (AEZ) and resource based development generating
location-specific technologies. This would also help achieve a more balanced development
between the regions. In addition, R&D activities would focus on market oriented and demand
driven technologies to serve the changing needs of both farmers and consumers for higher
quality and more diversified products.
3.    Within the regional framework, the changing agricultural research strategy aims to
generate location specific technologies using a farming systems approach (FSR). This will be
achieved by five main related activities:
*  regional problem identification through better research-extension-farmer linkages;
*  closer collaboration between research and extension to generate and package
technologies to meet local farmers' needs;
* technology transfer which would link researchers, extension workers and farmers
closer together;
*  organization of field days; and
*  production of materials and information which meet and recognize the absorptive
capacity and educational level of the farmers.
This departure from the strong sector and commodity based and centrally managed approach
to research, demands a closer collaboration between regional research staff and local
government staff (in particular extension), members of the private sector, (including CDOs),
representatives of farmer groups as well as creating stronger links with regional universities, for
specific research topics and training.



94                          ANNEX 2
4.    AARD has taken action to reorient the mandates of 15 national commodity research
institutes of which: five are involved in foodcrops; three in horticulture; three in industrial
crops; two in livestock; and three in fisheries. Most of the stations of these national research
institutes, together with the nearest agricultural support and service centers have been frilly
integrated into 17 new Assessment Institutes for Agricultural Technology (AIAT) under
AARD's Center for Agricultural Socio-Economic Research (CASER). Under ARMP II, the
overall implementation of the AIAT programs is managed by one central Project Management
Unit (PMU), headed by a central Director and assisted by a National Advisory Comnmittee.
Each AIAT is headed by a regional Director and supported by a small Project Implementation
Unit (PIU) under an ARMP II Sub-project Coordinator. The PIU Coordinator, supported by a
few AIAT staff, reports directly to the AIAT Director, who in tum reports to the ARMP II
Director. Only eight of the ALATs are Bank assisted under the ARM II Project, including the
Kendari AIAT in Sulawesi Tenggara and the Biromaru AIAT in Sulawesi Tengah. The
remaining 9 institutes will probably be financed directly by Government, ADB and other
donors.
The Sulawesi Regional Assessment Institutes for Agricultural Technology
5.    The two Sulawesi AIATs are now being strengthened and staffed by AARD research
staff, relocated from the national research stations to the regions, the two food crops sub-
stations of Wawotobi in Sulawesi Tenggara and Sidondo in Sulawesi Tengah, and their
respective Agricultural Information Centers (Balai Informasi Pertanian - BIP) in Kendari and
Biromaru. In each province there is a Regional Advisory Committee (RAC) chaired by the
head of each provincial BAPPEDA and co-chaired by the head of MOA's regional office
(Kanwil), with members drawn from various Dinas-Dinas, local universities, farmers' groups,
local CDOs and private sector. The RAC is expected to guide the AIAT and its Coordinator
on the research & development program, priorities and policies. It will be assisted by a
Regional Technical Working Group (RTWG) drawn from the various agencies. Since both
AIATs also have to cover their neighboring provinces, Sulawesi Tenggara for Sulawesi Selatan
and Sulawesi Tengah for Sulawesi Utara, both AIATs have two RACs and two RTWGs.
Both AIATs have recently prepared their draft research work-plan proposals for the coming
years, which have to be financed under ARNMP II, but still need to be approved by the central
PMU at AARD. One of the first AIAT activities under the ARMP II project will be a FSR
Baseline Survey to support area development farming systems and fisheries research and
technology transfer.
Support of the AIATs to the Proposed SAADP
6.    In addition to their own programs under ARMP II, both AIATs will support the
SAADP Project in certain areas of development in the two provinces. They will support the
Project in the following three main areas.
(a)   assistance to the Dinas-Dinas Food, Estate Crops, Livestock and Fisheries
extension agents in the transfer of proven agricultural technologies and in the
design of the most relevant agro-technological demonstrations on farmers fields



95                           ANNEX 2
as funded under the Project. The Dinas-Dinas extension agents will remain
responsible for the actual organization, lay-out and monitoring of the
demonstrations and organize farmers groups discussion meetings;
(b)    carrying out specific applied, priority research projects in support of regional
agricultural development and based on identified local specific needs; for this
purpose the project provides 6 years of program funding; and
(c)    establishment, with the assistance of DGEC, of new superior mother trees
stock gardens for cashew (Kendari) and cacao (Biromaru), 5 ha each,
estimated at RP 61 million/ garden
Specific funds will be made available for these three components under the SAADP Project to
the two AIATs in Kendari and Biromaru to support the Project.
A. Demonstrations of Crop Production Technologies
7.    The recommended basic two year demonstrations to be carried out under the Project
are:
8.    Village inigated. rice and soybean production systems: based on local farmers capacity
and needs. Project inputs will include: tested improved rice and soybean varieties, relevant
fertilizer inputs, basic pest control chemicals and some equipment. The main technical themes
will involve: proper land preparation, timely (trans)planting, proper crop husbandry and on-
farm water management, reduction of harvest losses, etc. For rainfed sawahs, similar demos
can be carried out, using more adaptable, less risky hydromorphic varieties and reduced inputs.
9.    Dryland inter-cropped farming systems. Project inputs will include: tested improved
maize and mungbean varieties (including improved cassava cuttings where appropriate), high
quality cashew/other appropriate tree crop (including fruit tree) and leguminous tree seedlings,
king grass (for cattle fodder) and vetiver grass (to reduce/control erosion), fertilizers and
limited pesticides and some equipment. Demonstrations to be laid out on flat and steeper
sloping lands. Common forms of dryland farming techniques are: alley cropping (tanaman
lorong), contour ridge terracing (teras guludan), cover crops, living fences (pagar hidup) and
bench terraces (teras bangku).
10.   Rehabilitation of overgrown/closed in cashew farms. These demonstrations will mainly
be carried out in Sulawesi Tenggara, in particular on the islands of Muna and Buton. Mature
closed tree stands will be thinned out to some 33 trees/ha and interplanted with foodcrops and
new improved cashew seedlings, which at a much later stage will replace the older cashew
trees. Inputs: seeds, seedlings, fertilizers, some pesticides and equipment. Top grafting of
young seedlings and coppiced unproductive trees with superior clonal material will also be
demonstrated.



96                          ANNEX 2
11.   Rehabilitation of cacao under coconut or other mixed trees. For cacao, it is
recommended to apply proper pruning techniques to improve tree shape, fruit bearing and crop
sanitation and to improve the quality of the shade trees, e.g. larntoro and gamal.
Recommended densities for mature trees is 400 to 500 trees/ha. Inputs are: cacao seedlings
and leguminous shade tree seedlings for replacement of old trees, fertilizers, some pesticides
and equipment.
12.   Improvement of House Plots and Home Gardens. On average village house plots/home
gardens are about 0.2 ha and planted with a variety of crops (including vegetables) and fruit
trees. Under the Project, these gardens will be improved to increase production and their
nutritive and economic value. Better yielding fruit trees, vegetables and cash crops like vanilla,
pepper and ginger will be made available from the village nurseries through the PKK in the
village. The project will establish about 2 home garden improvement demos per village or 0.4
ha/village. One of the aspects of improved home gardening is to maximize the use of vegetative
waste and cow manure into garden compost. Costs per home garden demonstration is
estimated at RP 0.1 million, or RP 0.5 million/ha.
B. Applied Priority FSR Program
13.   The following applied research areas for the two AIATs, have been identified as
relevant to the Sulawesi Tenggara and Tengah SAADP Project:
(a)  Farming Systems Research on Cattle Fattening, making better use of crop
residues from dryland farms and leguminous trees clippings;
(b)  cashew research, including the introduction of vegetative multiplication, mainly
by the Kendari AIAT;
(c)  cacao research, including setting up a biological control/IPM program, mainly by
the Biromaru AIAT;
(d)  Fisheries and Coastal Ecosystems Research;
14.   Although the SAADP draft Project Implementation Plan (PIP) provides a long list of
research projects in the above four categories, none of the AIATs or local government staff
met is familiar with the proposals. It is therefore recommended that during the first project
year, these research project proposals be reviewed in light of the ARMP II, FSR Baseline
Survey results (which should be completed by that time) and finalized by the two AIATs
themselves at two regional research workshops to be organized with the Regional Technical
Working Groups. Following the workshop's recommendations, and based on the actual
available manpower and capability at the two Institutes and supporting agencies, a realistic
research implementation plan for both AIATs will be developed. Such an approach will allow
for a better distribution of research tasks among the two AIATs and prevent overlapping, thus
reducing costs. To support the two regional FSR workshops, the SAADP Project would
provide 2 man-months of technical assistance to help the AIAT's to establish their research



97                           ANNEX 2
priorities, in particular that of their FSR applied research program and to develop their research
implementation plans. Both institutes would receive additional intemational assistance in
developing their specific cashew and cacao research programs.
C. The Establishment of Superior Cashew and Cacao Mother Tree Gardens.
15.   Cashew. Sulawesi Tenggara is the main cashew producing province in Indonesia with
some 125,000 ha. Cashew plantings are concentrated in the lower rainfall areas, especially on
the islands of Muna and Buton. This crop has only been planted extensively by smalthoders
during the last ten to fifteen years and spacing, inter-cropping, management, and processing
practices are not always in accordance with technical recommendations. Initially, cashew
plantings were recommended by the Government to improve erosion control on denuded and
exhausted sloping and critical lands and to rehabilitate watersheds. Seeds and seedlings were
collected from anywhere to follow the planting rush. As a consequence, cashew tree stands are
very heterogeneous with all types of trees with different qualities and yield potentials. In some
areas, smallholders have established monocrop cashew plantings on land traditionally used for
foodcrops in response to the Government's promotion of cashew and have then cultivated
marginal uplands instead.
16.   The international market for cashew has been expanding continuously over the past 20
years. Therefore, as a most suitable industrial smallholders' crop, cashew has comparative
advantages in supporting national policies for agricultural development, particularly in the
Eastem part of Indonesia. Development of this crop could well contribute to increase farmers'
incomes, export earnings and agro-industrial development. Unfortunately, because of the
heterogeneity of the trees, productivity in Indonesia is relatively low in comparison with
production levels in Brazil and neighboring countries, such as India, Thailand, Vietnam and
Northern Australia.
17.   Although Indonesia has a major research center for most commercial treecrops, there is
no special research institute in Indonesia which deals particularly with cashew and the above
described problems. Consequently, it is recommended that under the SAADP Project, AIAT
Kendari gradually become a main cashew research activity center. To start this initiative, and
with the assistance of the Dinas for Estate Crops, a 5 ha superior mother tree garden for
cashew will be established in South Kendari under the SAADP Project, from where selected
clonal materials will eventually be further disseminated to Dinas-Dinas and village nurseries.
Because cashew can be easily vegetatively multiplied by top grafting or otherwise, the
establishment of the clonal seed garden and the dissemination of vegetative material can be
done relatively quickly. Besides financing a survey to identify potential high yielding and
tolerant cashew mother trees in farmers' fields in Sulawesi Tenggara, the Project would finance
(2 months) technical assistance for an international cashew specialist to assist AIAT Kendari
and collaborating Dinas Estate Crops in setting up such a superior mother trees stock garden
and provide guidance for developing its cashew research program. Total costs of the survey are
estimated to be RP 10 million, mutually covered by the project budgets of the provincial Dinas
Estate Crops and AIAT Kendari.



98                           ANNEX 2
18.   Cacao. Cacao is one of the main commercial tree crops in Sulawesi, with the two
project provinces Sulawesi Tenggara and Sulawesi Tengah in particular having a combined
area of some 95,000 ha. The provinces are rapidly expanding their cacao production and are
presently contributing more than 20% of the total national production. In particular Sulawesi
Tengah has a great potential for expansion. In comparison with other cacao producing areas in
Indonesia, Sulawesi is encountering its own production problems, due to poor quality of
seed/plant stock materials, the spread of the Cacao Pod Borer (CPB) disease from the North,
Helopeltis and the vascular streak dieback (VSD) disease. One of the recomnmendations by
MOA is to eradicate affected trees and condomize the fruits. However no farmer is willing to
cut down his cacao trees. Another problem are the heavy metals in many soils in Sulawesi,
affecting the quality of the cacao beans and butter. Cultural practices (especially proper pruning
and pest control) by local farmers are frequently less than adequate, since many farmers still
consider cacao as a "forest" crop, that needs to be harvested only. Like in the case of cashew,
research and extension support for cacao is inadequate and needs to be strengthened.
19.   The main cacao research institute in Indonesia is in Jember, East Java. Considering
however, the many new problems in production and expansion of cacao in Sulawesi, combined
with the different agro-ecological environments, the experience and knowledge of the Jember
cacao research institute may not be sufficient to answer the cacao production problems in
Sulawesi. Therefore it is strongly recommended that with the assistance from Jember, AIAT
Biromaru starts playing a much bigger role in cacao research for the Eastem Islands (including
Halmahera in Maluku), in the areas of improvement of seed and clonal stocks and the
development of biological control/IPM practices on farmers fields. Therefore, under the
SAADP Project, a start will be made in establishing a realistic cacao research program for
AIAT Biromaru and to establish a 5 ha superior mother tree garden in Sulawesi Tengah. The
Project will provide international technical (2 man-months) assistance to AIAT Biromaru to
develop the research program and to establish the mother trees garden (estimated costs: RP 61
million). Like in the case of cashew, Dinas Estate Crop will assist in the farmers' fields survey
to identify potential tolerant and well producing mother trees. Total costs of the survey are
estimated to be RP 10 million, mutually covered by the project budgets of the provincial Dinas
Estate Crops and AIAT Biromaru.
20.   Intemational Technical Assistance Required:
Farming Systems Specialist: 4 mm over Yr. 2, Yr. 3, and Yr. 4.
Int. Cashew Specialist : 4 mm over Yr. 2, Yr. 3, and Yr. 4.
Int. Cacao Specialist   4 nmm over Yr. 2, Yr. 3, and Yr. 4.
Two extra man-months for the FSR specialist are required to review the initial results of the
ARMP II FSR baseline surveys in both provinces and organize two regional research
workshops (estimated at RP 20 million each) with the assistance of the respective AIATs and
AARD. Total requirements for internationally experienced TA specialists: 20 mm.

PROJECT SUPERVISION PLAN
The mix of specialties and frequency and duration of missions likely to be needed to
ensure adequate project supervision is listed below. In addition, the Bank's Resident Mission
(RSI) would conduct ex-post reviews of works completed and goods supplied at the rate of
about two to three staff-weeks per year. RSI will also provide some support for explaining
procurement and disbursement arrangements during the project launch workshop.
